HERMETICA II
This volume presents in new English translations the scattered fragï¿¾ments and testimonies regarding Hermes Thrice Great that complete
Brian Copenhaverâ€™s translation of the Hermetica (Cambridge ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™…).
It contains the twenty-nine fragments from Stobaeus (including the
famous KorÄ“ Kosmou), the Oxford and Vienna fragments (never
before translated into English), an expanded selection of fragments
from various authors (including Zosimus of Panopolis, Augustine,
and Albert the Great), and testimonies about Hermes from thirtyï¿¾eight authors (including Cicero, Pseudo-Manetho, the Emperor
Julian, Al-KindÄ«, Michael Psellus, the Emerald Tablet, and Nicholas
of Cusa). All translations are accompanied by introductions and notes
which cite sources for further reading. These Hermetic texts will
appeal to a broad array of readers interested in western esotericism
including scholars of Egyptology, the New Testament, the classical
world, Byzantium, medieval Islam, the Latin Middle Ages, and the
Renaissance.

Preface
Despite widespread interest in the Hermetica across the globe, currently
there does not exist a reliable and up-to-date English translation of the
various Hermetic fragments and testimonies. Indeed, some of these fragï¿¾ments and testimonies remain generally unknown.
At the turn of the twentieth century, G. R. S. Mead made a translation
of select fragments into Victorian English from now-outdated editions.
The translation of the fragments by Walter Scott (ï™„ï™Œï™…ï™‡) was based on his
own re-written Greek text â€“ a jungle of emendations and transpositions.
When it came to testimonies, moreover, Scott did not actually translate the
Greek or Latin texts. He only rendered into English (via Latin translations)
texts originally written in Arabic.
The present translation serves a new generation of scholarly and lay
readers of the Hermetica. It remains faithful to â€“ though critically engaged
with â€“ the various manuscript traditions. Copious notes provide historical
background, parallels, and references for further reading. Included also are
many testimonies that Scott did not print. It is hoped that this volume will
spark interest in the full reception history of the Hermetica, which must
include Late Antiquity and the medieval period.
Here I gratefully acknowledge persons who read parts of the manuscript
and offered helpful suggestions: Brian P. Copenhaver, David Runia, and
Christian H. Bull. Christian Wildberg and Kevin Van Bladel also kindly
answered my inquiries and provided guidance based on their expertise. My
thanks also to Oxford University Press for the use of Van Bladelâ€™s translaï¿¾tions of Arabic source materials.

General Introduction
There are five groups of philosophical Hermetic writings that do not
appear in Brian P. Copenhaverâ€™s ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™… English translation entitled Hermeï¿¾tica: The Greek â€œCorpus Hermeticumâ€ and the Latin â€œAsclepiusâ€.
ï™„ The first is
the large group of Hermetic excerpts from Stobaeus, an early fifth-century
ï£ï¥ anthologist. The second is the Coptic Hermetica (discovered in ï™„ï™Œï™‡ï™ˆ)
featuring two excerpts of previously known writings in addition to a
formerly unknown Hermetic tractate (the Discourse on the Eighth and
Ninth). The third group is the collection of Hermetic Definitions, a set
of maxims extant in Greek fragments but preserved only fully in Armeï¿¾nian. The fourth is the previously known set of diverse fragments quoted
by various (largely Christian) authors in Late Antiquity. The fifth comï¿¾prises recently discovered Hermetic fragments currently preserved in
Vienna and Oxford.

It is high time to present a new translation and annotation of these (chiefly
philosophical) Hermetica to the English-speaking world.
Hermes-Thoth
Nothing binds together the multifarious Hermetic fragments beyond their
ascription to Hermes Thrice Great. Hermes Thrice Great is a fictional
character. Yet for many in the ancient world that fiction was history. If we
call Hermes Thrice Great a â€œmyth,â€ we thereby recognize that he is greater
and more significant than any one historical figure. How do we introduce a
figure that appears in so many different ages in so many different guises? If
there was an â€œoriginalâ€ Hermes Thrice Great, we are obliged to pick up the
thread at significant points of reception.
Iamblichus (about ï™…ï™‡ï™ˆâ€“ï™†ï™…ï™ˆ ï£ï¥) commences his book (later called On
the Mysteries) with the following flourish:
Hermes, the deity who presides over rational discourses, has long and
rightly been considered common to all who practice the sacred arts. He
who presides over true science concerning gods is one and the same
throughout the universe. It is to him that our ancestors dedicated the
discoveries of their wisdom, attributing all their own writings to Hermes.ï™‡
Important here is the frank acknowledgement that many authors wrote
under the name of Hermes. The practice of pseudepigraphy was logical for
devotees of Hermes.ï™ˆ True wisdom and learning merited ascription to the
lord of all learning. This is why many Egyptian scholars attributed their
writings to Hermes. Iamblichus, himself writing under a false name
(â€œAbammon,â€ an Egyptian priest), calls these writers his â€œancestors.â€ These
â€œancestorsâ€ were probably Hellenized Egyptian scribes and priests who
lived not very long before Iamblichus himself.
Later, Iamblichus gives a taste of how many persons were writing under
the name of Hermes. In On the Mysteries ï™‹.ï™„, he passes on the report of a
certain Seleucus, who attributed to Hermes a total of ï™…ï™ƒ,ï™ƒï™ƒï™ƒ books.
A better-known source, the Egyptian priest and historian Manetho, nearly

The many genres of Hermetic learning are well illustrated by a passage

Finally, the Singer held in his hands a songbook and an instruction manual
for kings. As a supplement, six books on medical matters (anatomy,
medicines, medical instruments, and gynecology) were displayed.ï™Œ All this
vast store of knowledge was ascribed to Hermes.

helmeted with the cap of invisibility, wielding the twisted caduceus with
the power to put even Argus with his hundred eyes to sleep.ï™„ï™ƒ The
Egyptian Hermes, on the other hand, was Thoth, depicted as the ibisï¿¾headed scribe of the gods, secretary of Re, giver of oracles, master of magic,
lord of the moon often appearing in the form of a dog-faced baboon.
When the Greeks dominated Egypt, they identified the Egyptian god
Thoth with Hermes. But why? What did they see in these two gods that
was similar?
There are several overlaps, yet we will focus on two.ï™„ï™„ First of all,
Hermes as psychopomp, or escort of the dead, resembled Thoth as seen in
the various versions of the Egyptian Book of the Dead (or the Book of Going
Forth by Day), chapter ï™„ï™…ï™ˆ. In the Hall of Two Truths, Thoth hears the
confession of the deceased person. After the personâ€™s heart is weighed
against the feather of Maat (â€œJustice,â€ or â€œTruthâ€), Thoth carefully
inscribes the result with his tablet and stylus. If the heart is pure, Thoth
leads the candidate into the presence of Osiris, the ultimate judge of the
dead, and finally into the Field of Reeds.ï™„ï™… Perhaps the most famous scene
of Hermes leading souls is the opening of Homerâ€™s Odyssey, book ï™…ï™‡. In
this scene, Hermes guides the freshly slaughtered suitors of Penelope to the
netherworld. Mindless, the suitors squeak like bats on their way to the
halls of Hades.
Yet there was a more basic similarity between Hermes and Thoth.
Hermes is more than a herald or messenger. He is the Logos â€“ the Reason,
Speech, or Word of God.ï™„ï™† The Word devises speech and brings his own
interpretation, which the Greeks called hermeneia.
ï™„ï™‡ Thoth is also the god
who presides over speech and interpretation. He is called â€œthe heart of Re,
the tongue of Atum, the throat of the God whose name is hidden.â€ As

In the Hellenistic period (roughly ï™†ï™…ï™†â€“ï™†ï™„ ï¢ï£ï¥), Greeks living in Egypt
and Hellenized Egyptians crafted an amalgam of Thoth and Hermes who

(Trismegistos, Termaximus). This Hermes is superlatively great in a superlaï¿¾tive (threefold) way. Following English convention, however, here we call
â€œThrice Greatest Hermesâ€ simply â€œHermes Thrice Great.â€
Greeks typically conceived of Hermes Thrice Great in a Euhemeristic
fashion. That is to say, they often considered him to have been an ancient
man â€“ a real scribe of a real Pharaoh, often the first divine Pharaoh called
Ammon (the Egyptian god Amun). This scribe, named Thoth or Theuth,
invented the alphabet and the art of writing.ï™„ï™‰ Ever since, humans have
been using writing to preserve the vast array of accumulated knowledge.
Thoth was later deified to become a recognized Egyptian god or daimon (a
kind of mediating deity).ï™„ï™Š Writing was the best-known benefit that
Thoth offered to human beings, but it was not the only one.
Greeks attributed to Thoth the invention of a host of other arts. Plato

dancing, and the culture of the olive.ï™„ï™Œ The Jewish writer Artapanus (third

[L8] Introduction
[L9] The Vienna Hermetica consist of four fragments on the backside of two
[L10] papyri housed in Vienna (P. Graec. Vindob. ï™…ï™Œï™‡ï™ˆï™‰ recto and ï™…ï™Œï™‹ï™…ï™‹ recto).
[L11] They belong to a single roll papyrologically dated to the end of the second
[L12] or the beginning of the third century ï£ï¥. The fragments attest a Hermetic
[L13] collection of at least ten tractates, the ninth called â€œOn Energies.â€ They
[L14] were initially published by H. Oellacher in ï™„ï™Œï™ˆï™„.
[L15] ï™„ In ï™„ï™Œï™‹ï™‡, Jean-Pierre
[L16] MahÃ© published an improved text with commentary.ï™…
[L17] On the front side of the papyri are fragments of the Jewish romance
[L18] called Jannes and Jambres. These two characters are magicians said to have
[L19] opposed Moses in Egypt.ï™† The Jewish text on the front of the papyrus and
[L20] the Hermetic text on the back may represent two different stages in the use
[L21] of the papyrus. Usually, the back of the papyrus was inscribed first. If the
[L22] writing on the back became effaced or the owner of the roll wanted to copy
[L23] out another text, the front of the papyrus was sometimes used. If so, the
[L24] Hermetic text on the back was the first text inscribed on the papyrus. Not
[L25] long afterward, it seems, the other side was used to copy out the Jewish
[L26] romance.
[L27] Possibly the owner of the papyrus desired both these texts for his or her
[L28] library. The owner was not necessarily Jewish, but apparently someone
[L29] interested in both (para-)biblical texts and Egyptian wisdom.

Assistant

What Hermetica II adds to the study of Hermeticism is not simply â€œmore texts,â€ but a different map of the tradition: one that shows how Hermetic teaching circulated in anthologies, papyri, school notebooks, Christian polemic, lateâ€“antique philosophy, Arabic science, and medieval speculation. Read alongside Copenhaverâ€™s Corpus Hermeticum and Asclepius, these materials change both the scale and the texture of what we mean by â€œHermeticism.â€

Where Copenhaver gives us a relatively coherent philosophical-theological corpus, Hermetica II exposes a much messier, more socially embedded, and historically mobile body of literature.

1. The Stobaean Hermetica (SH 1â€“29)

Context


These texts survive only because they were excerpted by John Stobaeus, a fifth-century CE anthologist compiling a massive florilegium for his son. Stobaeus was not interested in preserving Hermeticism as a tradition; he mined it for morally and philosophically useful material.

This means the Hermetic writings here appear as school texts, circulating alongside Plato, Aristotle, the Stoics, and the tragedians.

Contents


The Stobaean excerpts include:
  • Cosmogonies and anthropogonies

  • The doctrine of divine Nous and human intellect

  • The role of Fate (Heimarmene) and Providence

  • The structure of the cosmos and planetary powers

  • The nature of soul, embodiment, and ascent

  • The famous KorÄ“ Kosmou (â€œThe Daughter of the Cosmosâ€), a long mythic-philosophical narrative about the animation of the world and the fall of souls into bodies


The KorÄ“ Kosmou alone is longer than most individual treatises in the Corpus Hermeticum and represents a full mythic cosmology rather than a short dialogue or sermon.

Contributions beyond Copenhaver

Copenhaverâ€™s volume presents Hermeticism mainly as a set of philosophical dialogues oriented around salvation through knowledge. The Stobaean texts show something broader:

Hermeticism as a cosmological science.

Here Hermes is not merely a revealer of divine knowledge but a teacher of:

  • cosmic mechanics

  • astral causation

  • biological generation

  • natural law

  • planetary influence on psychology


We see Hermeticism functioning as a Hellenistic natural philosophy, deeply entangled with Stoicism, Platonism, medicine, and astrology. Nature appears as a rational, generative system governed by divine intellect and mathematical proportion. Humanity is generated according to the same principles that generate the cosmos itself.

This material also shows that Hermetic myth could operate on the same level as Platoâ€™s Timaeus: as speculative cosmology, not merely religious exhortation.

2. The Oxford Hermetica (OH 1â€“5)

Context


These fragments were discovered in a medieval Greek manuscript in the Bodleian Library and published only in 1991. The manuscript is a Christian anthology, and the Hermetic material appears alongside patristic excerpts and biblical scholia.

The Hermetic texts are therefore preserved in a Byzantine educational environment.

Contents


The Oxford fragments treat:
  • the soul and its faculties

  • sensation and perception

  • law and moral order

  • embryology and generation

  • psychology and mental activity


They resemble the Stobaean material in tone but are more technical and concise.

Contributions beyond Copenhaver

These fragments show Hermeticism functioning as a technical philosophical discipline, not only as revelation literature.

Here Hermes teaches:

  • how the senses work

  • how thought operates

  • how embryos develop

  • how law mirrors cosmic order


This is a Hermes of scientia, not only gnosis.

Copenhaverâ€™s Hermes is a preacher of divine awakening. The Oxford Hermes is a philosopher of mind and body. These texts reveal that Hermeticism was part of the same intellectual ecosystem as Galenic medicine, Aristotelian psychology, and Stoic physics.

3. The Vienna Hermetica (VH 1â€“2)

Context


These are papyrus fragments from a roll dated to the late second or early third century CE. They represent the earliest physical witnesses to a Hermetic book collection.

The roll originally contained at least ten Hermetic tractates, one titled On Energies. On the other side of the papyrus is a Jewish romance about Jannes and Jambres, the magicians who opposed Moses.

This juxtaposition is extraordinarily revealing.

Contents


Only four fragments survive, but they attest:
  • a structured Hermetic collection

  • treatises organized by topic

  • philosophical discussions of divine power (energeiai)


Contributions beyond Copenhaver

These papyri show Hermeticism as a book tradition, not merely a loose set of dialogues.

They prove that:

  • Hermetic treatises circulated in collected editions

  • readers curated Hermetic texts alongside Jewish and biblical literature

  • Hermeticism belonged to the same religious-intellectual marketplace as early Judaism and Christianity


Copenhaverâ€™s volume is silent about how Hermetic texts circulated physically. The Vienna papyri give us the archaeology of Hermetic reading.

4. Hermetic Fragments from Various Authors (FH 1â€“45)

Context


These fragments survive because they were quoted â€” often polemically â€” by Christian, Neoplatonic, and medieval authors from the third to the fifteenth centuries.

The citers include:

  • Tertullian

  • Lactantius

  • Cyril of Alexandria

  • Augustine

  • Zosimus of Panopolis

  • Iamblichus

  • Albert the Great

  • Nicholas of Cusa


Most of them were not neutral transmitters. They excerpted Hermes in order to argue with him, appropriate him, or subordinate him to Christian theology.

Contents


The fragments cover:
  • theology and cosmology

  • demonology

  • astral causation

  • fate and providence

  • creation doctrines

  • ethics and divine law


Zosimus preserves alchemical Hermetica. Cyril preserves philosophical theology. Lactantius treats Hermes as a pagan witness to Christian truth.

Contributions beyond Copenhaver

This material gives us the reception history of Hermeticism.

Copenhaverâ€™s volume ends in Late Antiquity. Hermetica II shows:

  • Hermes as a pagan prophet in Christian apologetics

  • Hermes as a rival theologian attacked by Church Fathers

  • Hermes as an authority in Islamic astrology and alchemy

  • Hermes as a prisca sapientia figure in the Latin Middle Ages


In short, it reveals how Hermeticism became one of the major genealogical authorities of Western esotericism.

It is here that Hermes becomes:

  • the founder of alchemy

  • the father of astrology

  • the origin of talismanic magic

  • the ancient theologian of natural law


None of this is visible in the Corpus Hermeticum alone.

5. The Testimonies Concerning Hermes Trismegistus (TH 1â€“38)

Context


These are not Hermetic writings but ancient reports about Hermes: pagan, Christian, Jewish, Islamic, and medieval.

The witnesses include:

  • Cicero

  • Manilius

  • Philo of Byblos

  • Emperor Julian

  • Al-KindÄ«

  • AbÅ« Maâ€˜shar

  • Ibn an-NadÄ«m

  • Michael Psellus

  • the Emerald Tablet tradition


Contents


They portray Hermes as:
  • the inventor of writing

  • the founder of astronomy

  • the author of thousands of books

  • the teacher of Egyptian priests

  • the first philosopher

  • the originator of alchemy and talismanic science


Contributions beyond Copenhaver

These testimonies explain why Hermetic texts carried authority.

They show Hermes functioning as an author-function â€” a legendary name that guaranteed antiquity, legitimacy, and universality. Hermes is not merely a speaker in dialogues; he is the imagined source of all sacred science.

This is how Hermes becomes:

  • the transmitter of Egyptian wisdom

  • the bridge between paganism and monotheism

  • the ancestor of scientific tradition


Without these testimonies, the later history of Hermeticism â€” Ficino, Pico, Agrippa, Bruno, Dee â€” makes no sense.

What We Learn About Hermeticism from These Texts

Taken together, these materials force a major revision of the standard picture.

Copenhaverâ€™s Hermetica presents:

a philosophical-religious movement centered on salvation through divine knowledge.

Hermetica II reveals:

a trans-Mediterranean intellectual tradition encompassing cosmology, medicine, astrology, alchemy, theology, magic, psychology, and natural philosophy.

We learn that Hermeticism was:

  • not a single school, but a library of sciences

  • not marginal, but deeply embedded in elite education

  • not isolated, but intertwined with Judaism, Christianity, and Islam

  • not purely mystical, but rigorously cosmological and technical

  • not static, but continually reinterpreted for fifteen centuries


Most importantly, we learn that Hermeticism functioned as a theory of knowledge itself: a vision in which the structure of the cosmos, the operations of mind, the laws of nature, and the destiny of the soul form a single intelligible order governed by divine intellect.

That total vision â€” philosophical, scientific, theological, and mythic at once â€” is only visible when the Stobaean excerpts, papyri, fragments, and testimonies are read alongside the Corpus Hermeticum.

In that sense, Hermetica II does not merely supplement Copenhaverâ€™s volume. It completes the historical reality of Hermeticism. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn1file0îˆ‚turn1file3îˆ‚turn1file4îˆ‚turn1file1îˆ

[L12] Hermes-Thoth
[L13] Nothing binds together the multifarious Hermetic fragments beyond their
[L14] ascription to Hermes Thrice Great. Hermes Thrice Great is a fictional
[L15] character. Yet for many in the ancient world that fiction was history. If we
[L16] call Hermes Thrice Great a â€œmyth,â€ we thereby recognize that he is greater
[L17] and more significant than any one historical figure. How do we introduce a
[L18] figure that appears in so many different ages in so many different guises? If
[L19] there was an â€œoriginalâ€ Hermes Thrice Great, we are obliged to pick up the
[L20] thread at significant points of reception.
[L21] Iamblichus (about ï™…ï™‡ï™ˆâ€“ï™†ï™…ï™ˆ ï£ï¥) commences his book (later called On
[L22] the Mysteries) with the following flourish:
[L23] Hermes, the deity who presides over rational discourses, has long and
[L24] rightly been considered common to all who practice the sacred arts. He
[L25] who presides over true science concerning gods is one and the same
[L26] throughout the universe. It is to him that our ancestors dedicated the
[L27] discoveries of their wisdom, attributing all their own writings to Hermes.ï™‡
[L28] Important here is the frank acknowledgement that many authors wrote
[L29] under the name of Hermes. The practice of pseudepigraphy was logical for
[L30] devotees of Hermes.ï™ˆ True wisdom and learning merited ascription to the
[L31] lord of all learning. This is why many Egyptian scholars attributed their
[L32] writings to Hermes. Iamblichus, himself writing under a false name
[L33] (â€œAbammon,â€ an Egyptian priest), calls these writers his â€œancestors.â€ These
[L34] â€œancestorsâ€ were probably Hellenized Egyptian scribes and priests who
[L35] lived not very long before Iamblichus himself.
[L36] Later, Iamblichus gives a taste of how many persons were writing under
[L37] the name of Hermes.

Assistant

Below is a closer look at the witnesses to Hermes Trismegistus preserved in Hermetica II â€” who they are, why they cite Hermes, and what kind of Hermes they construct. What emerges is not a single figure but a sequence of Hermes-images shaped by the needs of pagan philosophy, Christian polemic, Islamic science, and medieval natural magic.

These testimonies are not marginal curiosities. They are the machinery by which Hermes becomes one of the central ancestors of Western esotericism.

The nature of the witnesses

The â€œtestimonies concerning Hermes Thrice Greatâ€ (TH 1â€“38) are ancient and medieval reports about Hermes rather than writings by him. They range from the Hellenistic period through the Renaissance and come from pagan philosophers, Christian theologians, astrologers, historians, alchemists, and Islamic encyclopedists.

As Litwa notes, the collection is not exhaustive. There exist dozens more Arabic, Latin, alchemical, astrological, and magical texts that invoke Hermes, many of which remain unedited or poorly studied. Hermes appears everywhere in technical literature, often as a legendary authority or primordial teacher. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn2file1îˆ

The witnesses show that Hermeticism cannot be reduced to the philosophical dialogues translated by Copenhaver. Instead, Hermes becomes:

  • founder of astronomy and astrology

  • inventor of writing and law

  • first philosopher

  • master of alchemy and talismanic science

  • ancient Egyptian sage

  • pagan prophet anticipating Christianity

  • teacher of theurgists and magi


Pagan and Hellenistic witnesses

Artapanus (TH 1, 3rdâ€“2nd c. BCE)

Artapanus was a Jewish-Hellenistic writer who identified Moses with Hermes. In his account, Moses-Hermes teaches navigation, military engineering, philosophy, and technology. Hermes becomes the origin of civilization itself.

Here Hermes is not a mystic but a culture hero â€” inventor, lawgiver, engineer, and political founder. This anticipates later Islamic portrayals of Hermes as the first scientist.

Cicero (TH 2, 1st c. BCE)

Cicero reports that Hermes-Thoth gave the Egyptians their laws and letters. Hermes is presented as the inventor of writing and the foundation of political order.

This is the Euhemeristic Hermes: not a god but a divinized sage whose achievements explain the rise of civilization. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn2file3îˆ

Manilius (TH 3, 1st c. CE)

The Roman poet Manilius calls Hermes the founder of astrology â€” â€œthe first founder of this great and holy science.â€ Hermes is now the patriarch of astral knowledge.

This establishes the association between Hermes and the science of the stars that will dominate Islamic and medieval Hermeticism. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn2file3îˆ

Thrasyllus (TH 4, 1st c. CE)

Thrasyllus, astrologer to Emperor Tiberius, is the earliest known author to explicitly call Hermes Trismegistus. He helps crystallize the triple-great title that becomes canonical.

Here Hermes is an astronomical authority tied to imperial power.

Dorotheus of Sidon (TH 5, 1st c. CE)

Dorotheus, one of antiquityâ€™s greatest astrologers, treats Hermes as the primordial source of horoscopic astrology. Hermes stands at the beginning of technical astral science.

Philo of Byblos (TH 6, 2nd c. CE)

Philo presents Hermes as the inventor of writing and sacred science, embedded within Phoenician and Egyptian myth-history. His Hermes belongs to a lineage of ancient theologians and culture heroes.

Philosophical and theurgical witnesses

Iamblichus (TH 12, 3rdâ€“4th c. CE)

For Iamblichus, Hermes is the supreme theological authority and guide of theurgists. Hermes is the patron of ritual science and divine ascent.

Iamblichus explicitly states that many Egyptian priests wrote under the name of Hermes, and that true sacred science was always attributed to him. Hermes becomes an author-function: the name under which divine knowledge circulates. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn2file4îˆ

The Emperor Julian (TH 14, 4th c. CE)

Julian, the last pagan Roman emperor, treats Hermes as a divine philosopher and defender of traditional religion against Christianity. Hermes is part of Julianâ€™s attempt to revive pagan theology as a rational religious system.

Christian witnesses: Hermes as pagan prophet and rival theologian

Christian authors are among the most important witnesses because they quote Hermes extensively â€” often to refute him, but sometimes to appropriate him.

Litwa notes that most surviving Hermetic fragments come from Christian writers, especially Lactantius and Cyril of Alexandria. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn1file1îˆ

Tertullian (FH 1, early 3rd c.)

Tertullian is the first Latin Christian to mention Hermes. He calls Hermes â€œteacher of all the natural philosophers.â€ For him, Hermes is a rival authority whose prestige must be neutralized.

Lactantius (FH 3â€“15, early 4th c.)

Lactantius treats Hermes as a pagan witness to Christian truth. He quotes Hermes as teaching:

  • one supreme God

  • divine providence

  • the immortality of the soul

  • moral judgment


Hermes becomes a prisca theologus â€” an ancient pagan who unknowingly anticipated Christianity.

Cyril of Alexandria (FH 23â€“35, 5th c.)

Cyril preserves more Hermetic material than any other author. He quotes Hermes in order to refute pagan theology, but in doing so he preserves large sections of otherwise lost Hermetic treatises.

Through Cyril, we glimpse a sophisticated pagan theology rivaling Christianity in scope and ambition.

Augustine (FH 41; TH 19, late 4thâ€“early 5th c.)

Augustine presents Hermes as a historical Egyptian sage who was later deified. He accepts Hermesâ€™ antiquity but denies his divinity.

Augustineâ€™s account will shape medieval Christian views of Hermes for centuries.

Islamic witnesses: Hermes as prophet-scientist

In Islamic tradition, Hermes becomes a prophetic figure â€” sometimes identified with Idris (Enoch) â€” and the founder of astronomy, alchemy, and philosophy.

Al-KindÄ« (TH 25, 9th c.)

Al-KindÄ« treats Hermes as an authority on astrology and cosmology. Hermes stands at the beginning of scientific tradition.

AbÅ« Maâ€˜shar (TH 26, 9th c.)

AbÅ« Maâ€˜shar presents Hermes as the primordial sage who transmitted astral wisdom to humanity after the Flood.

This establishes Hermes as the ancestor of Islamic astral science.

Ibn an-NadÄ«m (TH 27, 10th c.)

In his bibliographic encyclopedia, Ibn an-NadÄ«m lists dozens of books attributed to Hermes. Hermes appears as a prolific scientific author.

Al-MubaÅ¡Å¡ir ibn FÄtik (TH 28, 11th c.)

He presents Hermes as a philosopher-king, lawgiver, and sage â€” part of a lineage of ancient wisdom figures.

Medieval and Renaissance witnesses

Michael Psellus (TH 29, 11th c.)

Psellus links Hermes with Zoroaster and Ostanes, the Persian magi. Hermes becomes part of a trans-cultural lineage of ancient magic and theology. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn2file2îˆ

The Emerald Tablet (TH 30)

The most famous Hermetic text of all: source of the dictum â€œas above, so below.â€ Here Hermes is the father of alchemy and the science of transformation.

This text becomes central to medieval and Renaissance alchemy.

Albert the Great (TH 36, 13th c.)

Albert treats Hermes as a natural philosopher and master of occult properties. Hermes becomes part of scholastic natural science.

Picatrix (TH 37, 11thâ€“13th c.)

In this Arabic grimoire, Hermes is the originator of talismanic magic and astral ritual.

Nicholas of Cusa (TH 38, 15th c.)

Nicholas treats Hermes as a prisca theologian whose teachings converge with Christian mysticism.

What the witnesses reveal about Hermeticism

From these testimonies, several conclusions follow:

1. Hermes is not just a philosopher â€” he is the ancestor of science.
He founds astrology, alchemy, medicine, magic, law, and theology.

2. Hermeticism is not marginal â€” it is civilizational.
Hermes stands at the beginning of writing, astronomy, kingship, and law.

3. Hermeticism is not purely mystical â€” it is technical.
Hermes teaches astronomy, talismans, medicine, chemistry, cosmology.

4. Hermes is a trans-religious figure.
He belongs to paganism, Christianity, Judaism, Islam, and Renaissance Platonism.

5. Hermes is an author-function.
His name legitimates knowledge by giving it immemorial antiquity.

In short, the witnesses show that â€œHermeticismâ€ is not merely a set of late antique philosophical dialogues. It is a civilizational myth of knowledge itself â€” the story of how divine wisdom entered human history through writing, science, ritual, and philosophy.

This Hermes is not only the revealer of gnosis.
He is the imagined founder of the sciences.

Assistant

What Hermetica II makes possible â€” and what Copenhaverâ€™s Corpus Hermeticum by itself cannot give you â€” is a prosopography of Hermesâ€™s readers: a long chain of philosophers, priests, theologians, astrologers, alchemists, and encyclopedists who invoke Hermes for very different purposes. These authors are not merely transmitters. Each constructs a different Hermes according to the intellectual economy of his own time.

What follows is a guided tour of the major authorial clusters and the kinds of Hermeticism they produce.

1. Pagan philosophers and historians


(Hermes as primordial sage, founder of science and civilization)

These authors establish the cultural authority of Hermes.

Artapanus (3rdâ€“2nd c. BCE)


A Jewish-Hellenistic historian writing in Greek. Artapanus identifies Moses with Hermes, transforming biblical history into Egyptian sacred history. Moses-Hermes invents navigation, war machines, philosophy, and political organization.

Here Hermes is a civilizational architect â€” not a mystic but a technologist and lawgiver. This is the earliest form of the Hermes-as-founder-of-science tradition.

Cicero (1st c. BCE)


In De natura deorum Cicero reports that Hermes-Thoth gave Egypt its laws and letters. Hermes is the inventor of writing and political order.

This is the Euhemeristic Hermes: a historical sage later divinized. Ciceroâ€™s Hermes stands at the origin of culture itself. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn2file3îˆ

Manilius (1st c. CE)


The Roman astrologer-poet calls Hermes the first founder of astrology, â€œthis great and holy science.â€

This is the first clear formulation of Hermes as patriarch of astral knowledge â€” a role that will dominate Islamic and medieval Hermeticism.

Thrasyllus (1st c. CE)


Astrologer to Emperor Tiberius. He is the earliest known writer to use the title Hermes Trismegistus explicitly. His Hermes is an astronomical authority tied to imperial power.

Dorotheus of Sidon (1st c. CE)


The most important technical astrologer of antiquity. Dorotheus treats Hermes as the ultimate authority behind horoscopic astrology. Hermes is now the patron saint of mathematical prediction.

2. Middle Platonists and Neoplatonists


(Hermes as divine theologian and theurgic authority)

Here Hermes becomes a metaphysician and ritual theorist.

Philo of Byblos (2nd c. CE)


Philo presents Hermes as inventor of writing and sacred theology embedded in Phoenician-Egyptian myth-history. Hermes becomes a figure of ancient revealed wisdom that precedes Greek philosophy.

Iamblichus (3rdâ€“4th c. CE)


The most important philosophical witness.

Iamblichus presents Hermes as the supreme theological authority and guide of theurgists. He explicitly states that Egyptian priests wrote their sacred books under Hermesâ€™ name and that all divine science was attributed to him.

Hermes is no longer merely an author â€” he is the divine patron of ritual philosophy and sacred science. Iamblichus treats Hermetic literature as part of the same world as Chaldaean Oracles, Platonic theology, and temple ritual. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn2file4îˆ

Emperor Julian (4th c. CE)


The last pagan Roman emperor, Julian presents Hermes as a divine philosopher defending traditional religion against Christianity. Hermes becomes a pillar of pagan theological resistance.

3. Christian theologians


(Hermes as rival theologian, pagan prophet, or corrupted sage)

Christian authors preserve more Hermetic material than anyone else â€” often unintentionally.

Litwa notes that thirty-nine of the forty-five Hermetic fragments come from Christian writers, especially Lactantius and Cyril. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn1file1îˆ

Tertullian (early 3rd c.)


The first Latin Christian to mention Hermes. He calls Hermes â€œteacher of all natural philosophers.â€

Hermes here is a rival intellectual authority whose prestige must be neutralized.

Lactantius (early 4th c.)


Lactantius treats Hermes as a pagan witness to Christian truth. He quotes Hermes teaching:

  • one supreme God

  • divine providence

  • moral judgment

  • immortality of the soul


Hermes becomes a prisca theologus â€” an ancient pagan who unknowingly anticipated Christianity.

Cyril of Alexandria (5th c.)


The most important Christian transmitter of Hermetic material. Cyril quotes Hermes extensively in order to refute pagan theology. In doing so, he preserves long sections of lost Hermetic treatises.

Through Cyril we glimpse a pagan philosophical theology fully competitive with Christianity.

Augustine (late 4thâ€“early 5th c.)


Augustine presents Hermes as a historical Egyptian sage later deified. He accepts Hermesâ€™ antiquity but denies his divinity. This Euhemeristic Hermes becomes standard in medieval Christianity.

4. Alchemists and technical writers


(Hermes as master of hidden nature)

Here Hermes becomes the father of occult science.

Zosimus of Panopolis (3rdâ€“4th c.)


The earliest known alchemist. Zosimus treats Hermes as the source of alchemical revelation and links him with Persian magi such as Ostanes and Zoroaster.

Hermeticism here becomes chemical, metallurgical, and soteriological at once.

The Greek Magical Papyri


Hermes appears as cosmic ruler, creator, healer, lord of the elements, and sender of oracles. He governs astral powers and natural forces. This is the ritual Hermes of Egyptian domestic religion.

5. Islamic philosophers and encyclopedists


(Hermes as prophet-scientist and founder of astronomy)

In Islam, Hermes becomes a prophetic figure â€” often identified with Idris (Enoch).

Al-KindÄ« (9th c.)


Treats Hermes as an authority on astrology and cosmology. Hermes becomes part of the genealogy of scientific knowledge.

AbÅ« Maâ€˜shar (9th c.)


Presents Hermes as the primordial sage who transmitted astral wisdom after the Flood. Hermes becomes a civilizational restart button.

Ibn an-NadÄ«m (10th c.)


In his bibliographic encyclopedia, Ibn an-NadÄ«m lists dozens of books attributed to Hermes. Hermes appears as a prolific scientific author whose corpus spans multiple sciences.

Al-MubaÅ¡Å¡ir ibn FÄtik (11th c.)


Hermes is portrayed as a philosopher-king and lawgiver â€” one of the ancient sages whose wisdom founded civilization.

6. Medieval and Renaissance thinkers


(Hermes as ancient theologian and natural magician)

Michael Psellus (11th c.)


Links Hermes with Zoroaster and the Persian magi. Hermes becomes part of a trans-cultural lineage of ancient magical theology. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn2file2îˆ

The Emerald Tablet


The most famous Hermetic text. Source of â€œas above, so below.â€ Hermes is now the father of alchemy and transformation.

Albert the Great (13th c.)


Treats Hermes as a natural philosopher and master of occult properties. Hermes is absorbed into scholastic science.

Picatrix


The great Arabic grimoire. Hermes is founder of talismanic magic and astral ritual.

Nicholas of Cusa (15th c.)


Treats Hermes as a prisca theologian whose metaphysics converges with Christian mysticism.

What these authors collectively show

From this chain of witnesses, Hermeticism appears not as a marginal cult or a set of late antique dialogues, but as a civilizational myth of knowledge:

Hermes is imagined as:

  • inventor of writing

  • founder of law

  • father of astrology

  • master of alchemy

  • source of ritual magic

  • teacher of philosophers

  • prophet of divine unity

  • ancestor of science


Copenhaver gives you Hermes as a philosopher of salvation.
Hermetica II gives you Hermes as the imagined founder of all sacred and scientific knowledge.

Hermeticism emerges not as a school, but as a genealogy of wisdom stretching from Egyptian temples to Renaissance laboratories.

Assistant

Here are 50 fun facts about the new picture of Hermeticism that emerges once you read Hermetica II alongside Copenhaver â€” the Hermes of libraries, laboratories, temples, palaces, monasteries, and observatories, not just philosophical dialogues.

This is Hermes not only as mystic teacher, but as the imagined founder of civilizationâ€™s sciences.

50 Fun Facts about the Expanded Hermetic Tradition

1. Hermes was believed to have written tens of thousands of books â€” Manetho credited him with 36,525 volumes, one for every day of a four-year Egyptian calendrical cycle.

2. In antiquity, Egyptian temple libraries were sometimes called the â€œHouse of Life,â€ and Hermes was believed to be their original author.

3. Hermes was thought to have invented writing itself â€” making him the ancestor of all literature, not just esoteric texts.

4. The famous title Hermes Trismegistus (â€œThrice Greatâ€) probably originated in Egyptian priestly epithets that were later translated into Greek.

5. The earliest author to use the title â€œTrismegistusâ€ is Thrasyllus, astrologer to Emperor Tiberius.

6. Hermes was believed to be the founder of astrology, not just a practitioner.

7. Roman poet Manilius called astrology a â€œholy scienceâ€ founded by Hermes.

8. In Islamic tradition, Hermes was often identified with Idris (Enoch), the prophet who ascended to heaven.

9. Hermes was believed to have survived the Flood and re-founded civilization afterward.

10. In Arabic sources, Hermes is sometimes described as a philosopher-king who ruled Egypt.

11. Hermes was credited with inventing navigation, war machines, and engineering.

12. Some Jewish writers identified Moses himself with Hermes.

13. Hermes was thought to be the inventor of law and the founder of political order.

14. Ancient Egyptians believed Hermes (Thoth) governed the judgment of the dead in the underworld.

15. Hermes was believed to be the cosmic scribe who recorded the fate of every soul.

16. In the Greek Magical Papyri, Hermes is invoked as creator of the cosmos.

17. Some hymns call Hermes the helmsman of the universe.

18. Hermes was thought to rule over the four elements.

19. Hermes was believed to control the hidden powers beneath heaven and earth.

20. Hermes was said to possess knowledge no god could fully behold.

21. Hermes was the patron god of alchemy centuries before the Emerald Tablet became famous.

22. The earliest alchemist, Zosimus of Panopolis, treats Hermes as the source of alchemical revelation.

23. Hermes was linked with Zoroaster and the Persian magi.

24. Hermes was part of a trans-cultural lineage of ancient sages including Ostanes, Pythagoras, and Plato.

25. Neoplatonists believed Plato studied the pillars of Hermes in Egypt.

26. Hermes was considered the supreme authority of theurgy (ritual ascent to the gods).

27. The philosopher Iamblichus said Egyptian priests wrote all sacred science under Hermesâ€™ name.

28. Hermes functioned as an ancient author-function â€” his name guaranteed authority.

29. Christian theologians treated Hermes as a pagan prophet who anticipated Christianity.

30. Lactantius quoted Hermes as teaching monotheism.

31. Hermes was sometimes portrayed as a teacher of divine providence.

32. Augustine accepted Hermes as a real Egyptian sage later deified.

33. Medieval monks preserved Hermetic fragments while trying to refute paganism.

34. The Emerald Tablet became the most famous alchemical text in Europe.

35. The phrase â€œas above, so belowâ€ originates from Hermetic tradition.

36. Hermes became the ancestor of talismanic magic.

37. The grimoire Picatrix presents Hermes as founder of astral ritual.

38. Hermes was believed to control planetary intelligences.

39. Hermes was associated with medicine and healing.

40. Hermes was believed to have written treatises on embryology.

41. Hermes taught psychology centuries before Aristotle.

42. Hermetic texts were copied into Byzantine school anthologies.

43. Hermetic texts circulated alongside biblical commentaries.

44. Hermetic papyri were reused for Jewish magical romances.

45. Hermes was read by Christian bishops, pagan emperors, Islamic astronomers, and medieval monks.

46. Hermes became one of the central figures of Renaissance magic.

47. Ficino believed Hermes was older than Moses.

48. Hermes became the foundation of the Renaissance idea of ancient wisdom (prisca sapientia).

49. Hermes was imagined as the ancestor of both science and religion.

50. Hermeticism turns out to be not a cult or sect, but a civilizational myth of knowledge itself.

The Big Picture

Copenhaver gives you Hermes as a philosophical mystic.
Hermetica II gives you Hermes as the imagined founder of:

  • writing

  • law

  • science

  • astrology

  • alchemy

  • magic

  • philosophy

  • theology


Hermeticism turns out to be the story Western civilization told itself about where knowledge came from.

And that story starts in Egyptian temples, travels through Roman palaces, Byzantine monasteries, Islamic observatories, and Renaissance laboratories â€” all under the name of Hermes Thrice Great. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn2file1îˆ‚turn2file2îˆ‚turn2file3îˆ‚turn2file4îˆ

INTRODUCTION
Born to a noble family in Chalcis ad Belum (in Coele-Syria, modern Qinnesï¿¾rin) c. 240ad, and studying with the Neoplatonic philosopher Porphyryâ€”
probablyâ€”in Rome, Iamblichus (á¾½Î™á½±ÂµÎ²Î»Î¹Ï‡Î¿Ï‚) established in his native Syria
a philosophical school which constituted an important link in the Golden
Chain of the Platonic tradition. His difficult and controversial works have
provoked a good deal of attention on the part of historians of philosophy
and religion and occupy a distinct place in modern scholarship. Hailed by
some as the most sublime and dazzling metaphysician who changed the
course of Platonism, he is deprecated by others as the most obscure though
prolific author, who imported into his texts all sorts of superstition, oriental
beliefs and magic, and eclectically fitted all this into his own bewildering
metaphysical schema with a heavy reliance on triadic subdivisions.
On his death in around 325ad, Iamblichus left to posterity a diverse body
of writings, some of which are still extant in their complete form, while othï¿¾ers are now available in the extracts preserved, most notably, in Stobaeusâ€™
vast Anthologia and in Neoplatonic commentaries. His writings influenced
the later Neoplatonists, such as Syrianus, Proclus and Damascius, while his
name became talismanic in the course of the pagan opposition to Christianï¿¾ity, most notably in the case of the Emperor Julian.
To the student of antiquity Iamblichus is perhaps best known as the
author of a treatise On the Pythagorean Way of Life, originally intended
to be an introduction to his â€˜Compendium to Pythagorean Doctrineâ€™ in
ten volumes, and now valued as a major source for our knowledge of the
Pythagorean tradition. Probably the most popular of Iamblichusâ€™ works, the
treatise is much studied and translated into modern languages.1
A treatise, On the Mysteries of Egypt, a defense of theurgy more properly
entitled A Reply of the priest Abammon to the letter of Porphyry to Anebo, and
the solutions to the questions it contains, is equally popular among students

4 introduction
Covering the totality of Iamblichean scholarship was certainly not our purï¿¾pose, but nonetheless the contributors managed to isolate and treat a numï¿¾ber of important issues, ranging from Pythagorean paideia to the metaï¿¾physics and hierarchy of virtues in Late Platonic philosophy.
The collection opens with two studies on the Pythagorean tradition.
Eugene Afonasin highlights the wealth of information on Pythagoras and
his tradition preserved in Clement of Alexandriaâ€™s Stromateis and presents
them against the background of Later Platonic philosophy. He first outlines
what Clement knew about the Pythagoreans, and then what he made of
the Pythagorean ideal and how he reinterpreted it for his own purposes.
Clement clearly occupies an intermediate position between the Neopyï¿¾thagorean biographical tradition, firmly based on Nicomachus, and that
more or less vague and diffuse literary situation which preceded the later
developments, and in this respect is a very good source, worth studying for
its own sake and as supplementary material which can help to understand
the great Pythagorean synthesis attempted by Iamblichus. Developing their
variants of the â€œexhortation to philosophyâ€ (protreptikoi logoi), these men
were much concerned with the educational value of the Pythagorean way
of life rather than biographical circumstances, designed to place the ancient
sage in the proper cultural context.
In his contribution to the volume, Luc Brisson first outlines the conï¿¾tent of Iamblichusâ€™ On the common mathematical science, the third book of
the ten-volume compendium of Pythagoreanism, envisaged and partially
accomplished by the Syrian Neoplatonist, and then offers a new translaï¿¾tion of an extract from the treatise (chapter 18), interesting in at least two
respects. The chapter is devoted to changes in the pedagogical technique,
allegedly introduced by the Pythagoreans in the teaching of mathematics:
namely, having taken numerology as a deductive system, they, according
to Iamblichus, perceived it as leading towards the intelligible realm, after
purification achieved by means of preliminary knowledge revealed in Ïƒá½»Âµï¿¾Î²Î¿Î»Î± and Î±á¼°Î½á½·Î³ÂµÎ±Ï„Î±. Thus Iamblichus introduces his famous picture of the
Pythagorean School, distinguishing, on the one hand, between the so-called
hearers (á¼€ÎºÎ¿Ï…ÏƒÂµÎ±Ï„Î¹ÎºÎ¿á½·) and the scientists, or disciples (ÂµÎ±Î¸Î·ÂµÎ±Ï„Î¹ÎºÎ¿á½·), whom
he identifies with those on the inside (Ï„Î¿á½ºÏ‚ Îµá¼´ÏƒÏ‰) and those on the outside
(Ï„Î¿á½ºÏ‚ á¼”Î¾Ï‰), and, on the other hand, equating the Ïƒá½»ÂµÎ²Î¿Î»Î± with á¼€ÎºÎ¿á½»ÏƒÂµÎ±Ï„Î±.
However anachronistic, this view was quite widespread in Late Antiquity
(cf. the previous study for greater details). Besides, Iamblichus shows how
â€œthe Pythagoreansâ€ derived the entire metaphysical structure from the Oneï¿¾Good and the first principles (Limit and Unlimited): they first produced
numbers, which, by means of participation, largely dependent on resem-

blance, were then placed in relation to genuine realities (Platoâ€™s Forms)
along with the rest of reality from the gods to matter.
Two subsequent contributions deal with the Letters of Iamblichus. John
Dillon starts with general observations on protreptic epistolography in Anï¿¾tiquity and notes that, of the Neoplatonists,Iamblichus appears to be among
those few who took interest in the possibilities offered by this genre. We
have no idea who collected the letters, but it well could be that Iamblichus
and his circle perceived them as a good introduction to philosophy for the
uninitiated. While the style of Iamblichusâ€™ more technical works in general
leaves much to be desired, the letters show the author of a lost treatise â€˜On
judging the best type of speechâ€™ as a reasonably good stylist. Asking what
makes a piece of writing a philosophical letter, Dillon answers tentatively
that, unlike a treatise in the form of a letter, a real letter must be personï¿¾alized, contextualized, and pitched firmly at the level of popular philosoï¿¾phy. Indeed, among the correspondents of Iamblichus one finds his pupils,
the members of local aristocracy, and friends, although some of the correï¿¾spondents cannot be identified with any certainty. The letters address the
ideas of political justice and right education and revolve around the concept
of fate. All forms of divine agency, from blind fate to personalized tychai,
Iamblichus derives from one general source, a certain â€œmost comprehensive
principle of causalityâ€ (Letter 8, fr. 1), ultimately responsible for all cosmic
order. Various means of instruction and, most notably, four forms of dialecï¿¾tical argumentation (Letters 5 and 13), should constitute the educational
system which leads to knowledge of this causal principle (Letter 14). Dialecï¿¾tical reasoning is the major milestone on the way of self-knowledge and
apprehension of true virtues, first addressed in general terms in Letter 16
and then specified in a series of letters, dedicated to such virtues, as arÃªte,
phronÃªsis, homonoia, andreia, and, on the contrary, akharistia. Although no
traces of a very detailed hierarchy of virtues, introduced by Iamblichus and
discussed by J. Finamore below in this volume, can be discerned in the letï¿¾ters, still â€œto the very culmination of all the virtues and the summation of
all of them â€¦ one can come being led by justiceâ€ (Letter 2, fr. 1), while â€œmulï¿¾tiformâ€ virtue of self-control brings about a suitable apportionment among
these of ruling and being ruled (Letter 3, fr. 1, and also 6, fr. 2). Good govï¿¾ernment depends on an advantageous combination of natural (Ï†á½»ÏƒÎµÎ¹) and
social (Î½á½¹Âµá¿³) factors, on the one hand, and the personality of the ruler and
his personal skills (Ï„á½³Ï‡Î½Î·) and luck (Ï„á½»Ï‡Î· ÎºÎ±á½¶ ÎºÎ±Î¹Ïá½¹Ï‚), on the other. Appropriï¿¾ate concord and á½ÂµÎ¿Î³Î½Ï‰ÂµÎ¿Ïƒá½»Î½Î· is a sign of good government (Letters 9 and
6). On the contrary, good social contract is undermined if the ruler is found
â€œin two minds toward himselfâ€ (Î´Î¹Ï‡Î¿Î³Î½Ï‰ÂµÎ¿Î½á¿¶Î½) (Letter 9).

6 introduction
The two-fold nature of the human personality (soul), one part of which is
affected by fate, while the other is free of its influence, is further discussed
by Daniela Taormina. She offers a very detailed analysis of two relevant pasï¿¾sages: the fragment of a letter addressed to Macedonius, On Fate (Letter 8,
Dillonâ€“Pollechtner) andDe mysteriis, VIII 6â€“7. Both texts link the individual
soul to two principles: one included within the order of fate, the other supeï¿¾rior to nature and free from the order of fate. The latter principle does not
make individual soul belong to the intelligible realm; nor does it infuse the
soul with the intelligible. Rather, it reflects the metaphysical view of particï¿¾ipation that Iamblichus adopts to describe the one-sided relation between
inferior and superior.
In her study of Neoplatonic metaphysics, Claudia Maggi asserts that
Iamblichusâ€™ doctrine of mathematical entities as metaxy follows from a
background mixing different traditions: not only the Neopythagorean one,
but also Platonic, Aristotelian and Plotinian ones. The hierarchy of numï¿¾bers, presented by the philosopher, probably lies not only with Neopyï¿¾thagorean models, but also with Aristotleâ€™s attribution to Plato of the docï¿¾trine that there are two kinds of separated numbers, the eidÂ¯etikoi and the
mathematical ones, and with Plotinusâ€™ numerical structure of being. The
Neopythagorean idea of the duplicity of archai mixes Old Academic docï¿¾trines, which can be traced back to Speusippus, and the Aristotelian notion
of intelligible matter, also used by Plotinus. Plotinusâ€™ influence on Iamï¿¾blichus is particularly visible through the doctrine of vertical causation.
One of the most peculiar aspects of Iamblichusâ€™ solutions is that they carry
out a synthesis capable of recovering, behind the name of Pythagoras, an
agreement underlying all ancient philosophical tradition, and of catching a
glimpse of such agreement in mathematical knowledge.
Gregory Shaw, who has done so much previously for conveying to the
scholarly world a true understanding of the nature and role of theurgy
in later Platonism, here contributes an insightful study of the status of
sense-perception (aesthesis) in theurgic practice. The gods of theurgy, after
all, penetrate the material realm with their influence, and theurgists are
concerned to engage and embody these gods by means of prayer and ritual.
This means that the aesthetic life of theurgists is necessarily the medium
through which they contact the gods. Far from escaping from the material
world and the senses, the theurgist employs aesthetic experience as the
necessary path to deification, and the vehicle through which this deification
occurs is the soulâ€™s subtle body, the ochÃªma. In theurgic ritual, the ochÃªma,
purified by daily prayer, is filled with the light of the gods and becomes
shining, augoeides: theurgists, in effect, become gods. The role of the ochÃªma
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 18 / 212>
introduction 7
is not to lift the soul out of the sublunary realm; rather, the light-filled
ochÃªma becomes simultaneously a vehicle for the descent of the god and
the deification of the soul. As Shaw emphasizes, it is important for us to
overcome our natural resistance to this way of looking at the theurgistâ€™s
experience, and recognize that there is much in their thought-world that
remains alien to us.
Next, John Finamore addresses the intriguing question of Iamblichusâ€™
contribution to the theory of the grades of virtue initiated by Plotinus,
specifically in his treatise I 2 [19]. Plotinus was initially seeking to address
the problem of the different, but analogous, ways in which the canoniï¿¾cal four virtues manifest themselves at various levels of human spiritual
progress. It is arguable that Plotinus was only proposing to distinguish
between two levels of virtue proper, the â€˜politicalâ€™ (of the soul as immersed
in civic affairs and the material life) and the â€˜purificatoryâ€™ or kathartikai,
proper to the philosophical soul that has diverted itself from worldly conï¿¾cerns. The â€˜paradigmatic virtuesâ€™ above these are properly paradigms of
virtue manifested in higher beings from pure souls up to gods. However that
may be, Porphyry, in s. 32 of his Sententiae, which is an exegesis of Enn. I 2,
chooses to discern fully four levels of virtue; and Iamblichus, in a treatise
On the Virtues (now lost), caps this by adding three more levels, one below
the Plotinian / Porphyrian levels (the â€˜naturalâ€™, proper even to irrational aniï¿¾mals), and two above, to accommodate the accomplished theurgist. All this
John Finamore discusses with great lucidity.
The significance of the role of divine providence, love and will in the phiï¿¾losophy of Iamblichus is the theme of the essay of Crystal Addey, as well
as his defense of the operation of theurgic prayer, religious invocation and
sacrifice. Recognizing that theurgy has all too often in the past been aligned
with magical practices, she argues that, on the contrary, the significance of
divine providence within Iamblichusâ€™ defense of theurgic prayer and reliï¿¾gious invocation serves to distinguish theurgy definitively from contempoï¿¾rary magical practices.
Svetlana Mesyats deals with the doctrine of divine henads, which she
accepts as being initially developed by Iamblichus, and identifies as arising,
in all probability, as the result of an exegesis of the first two hypotheses of
the Parmenides, in particular in relation to taking the predicates denied and
then asserted of the One as characteristics of different classes of henads. She
proposes a new reconstruction of Iamblichusâ€™ doctrine of henads, according
to which they are neither products of the One nor some lower substances
following after it, but rather different modes of its being a cause, insofar as
the One anticipates in itself this or that particular order of Being.

8 introduction
Lastly, Adrien Lecerf focuses on an interesting connection between Iamï¿¾blichus and the Emperor Julian. In the latterâ€™s Oration To the Mother of the
Gods, which is a philosophical interpretation of the myth of Cybele and
Attis, reference is made to an enigmatic â€˜third Demiurgeâ€™. Contrary to the
usual identification of this figure with the visible Helios, or to attempted
links with the theory of â€˜three demiurgesâ€™ of Amelius and Theodorus of
Asine, he suggests that it may be better to make a comparison with the
system of Demiurges to be discerned in Proclus, where we find a hierarchy
of Zeus, Dionysus and Adonis. Such a hierarchy of entities, he argues, on
the basis of parallels with Damascius, may well go back to Iamblichus,
and illuminate the revolution for which he is responsible in the field of
Neoplatonic theology.
It is to be hoped that this collection of papers will contribute to a furï¿¾ther deepening and refining of our appreciation of the contribution of
Iamblichus to the development of later Platonism.
The editors wish to thank the authors for their papers, Jacqueline Jones
who worked on the index for us, and the editors at Brill, especially Thalien
Colenbrander. Their hard work and diligence is much appreciated.

THE PYTHAGOREAN WAY OF LIFE IN
CLEMENT OF ALEXANDRIA AND IAMBLICHUS
Eugene Afonasin
Introductory Remarks
In his De communi mathematica scientia Iamblichus famously distinguishes
two orders of initiation within the Pythagorean School.1 However anachroï¿¾nistic, this distinction reflects a profound change of attitude to Pythagoreï¿¾anism which took place in the process of transition from the Late Hellenistic
to the Early Roman period.2
Clement of Alexandria as a â€˜Neopythagorean Philosopherâ€™ is relatively
badly served, however. It will be useful therefore to collect various obserï¿¾vations on this issue in a single outline. Clement is not only a good source,
which enhances our knowledge of the Pythagorean tradition. He also was
one of the first Christian philosophers to adopt the ancient theory of symï¿¾bolism and to sow it in the new Christian soil. In his works the conceptual
system of the second-century Middle Platonists and Neopythagoreans and
the method of allegorical exegesis of Philo of Alexandria were incorporated
1 76, 16ff. Festa. Cf. also De vita Pythagorica, 81. For text, translation and discussion see
the article by Luc Brisson, included in this volume.
2 As a part of the classical heritage, transmitted to Late Antiquity, the Pythagorean
tradition is relatively well documented by the extant sources, fragments and testimonia, and
much work has recently been done in the field. One can also observe the real renaissance
of interest to philosophical biography in recent scholarship. This is especially true about
the mysterious figure of Apollonius and the Neoplatonic philosophical biographies. The
subject in general is covered in M. Hadas and M. Smith (1965). Also consider the numerous
publications on Apollonius of Tyana, such as the progressive editions and translations of his
Letters, Eusebiusâ€™ polemical work and Philostratusâ€™ Bios (F. Conybeare 1950, R. Penella 1979,
and Ch. Jones 2005â€“2006), now classical monographs on Apollonius by M. Dzielska (1986)
and G. Anderson (1986), an account of scholarship on the subject by E. Bowie (1978), as well
as more recent studies by J.-J. Flinterman (1995) and Th. Schirren (2005).
Cf. also J. BollansÃ©e (1999) on Hermippos, as well as M. Edwards (1993 and 2000b), G. Clark
(2000) Ã‰. Des Places (1982), A.-J. FestugiÃ¨re (1937), J. Dillon and J. Hershbell (1991), G. Staab
(2002), Al. Oikonomides (1977), P. Athanassiadi (1999 and 2006) and D. Oâ€™Meara (1989 and
2006) on the Neoplatonic biographies by Porphyry, Iamblichus, Marinus, and Damaskï¿¾ius. One can also recollect studies on Diogenes Laertius and Hippolytus (A. Delatte 1922,
A.-J. FestugiÃ¨re 1945, B. Centrone 1992, and J. Mansfeld 1992).
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 25 / 212>
14 eugene afonasin
in the open texture of the Christian Weltanschauung. His distinction beï¿¾tween fundamental belief (koine pistis) and the highest faith, on the one
hand, and the scientific knowledge (episteme) and gnosis, on the other,
became fundamental for the later Christian theory of knowledge. The highï¿¾est faith and true gnosis were considered to be the final steps leading to
Gnostic perfection, and symbolism played the central role in the process
of its achievement. Clement believed that the student should be directed
and educated according to a certain model (partially cast, as I shall argue,
according to the Pythagorean paradigm). The education under the direction
of a learned instructor required time, ability to listen and understand, and a
special disposition towards knowledge, fortified by faith that the real knowlï¿¾edge could be achieved. In the process of paideia the student was supposed
to acquire a certain state of moral perfection, in a symbolic way learning
things, that could not be perceived otherwise, and exercising his analytical
ability by means of natural and precise sciences.
Clement is not unique in his interest in Pythagoreanism. It is quite probï¿¾able that, in his case, it was inherited from Philo (the best example being
a community of the Pythagorean type, described by Philo in his De vita
contemplativa), but equally possible is that the process went in both direcï¿¾tions: Philo, the Gnostics, Clement (and other Christian philosophers), on
the one hand, and Platonists like Nicomachus, Porphyry and Iamblichus, on
the other, more or less independently created an image that agreed with the
best ideals and expectations of the epoch. As a result, Clementâ€™s Pythagoras
resembles the true Gnostic, while the lives of Pythagoras and such â€˜Neoplaï¿¾tonic saintsâ€™, as Plotinus, Proclus or Isidorus are often reminiscent of the
Christian vitae and even the Gospels.3
Working with Clement I have found it useful to compare his approach to
the Pythagorean tradition with that of Iamblichus. The reasons, I believe,
will become clear below, but what should be mentioned at the outset is
that my interest is substantially based on the fact that, developing their
variants of the â€œexhortation to philosophyâ€ (protreptikoi logoi), these men
were much concerned with the educational value of the Pythagorean way of
life rather than (however important) biographical circumstances, designed
to place the ancient sage in the proper cultural context. Besides, Clement
3 A well known example is Iamblichus, De Vita Pythagorica, 12, where Thales is said to
proclaim â€˜good newsâ€™. J. Dillon and J. Hershbell (1991) rightly suspect a Christian influence

clearly occupies an intermediate position between the Neopythagorean
biographical tradition, firmly based on Nicomachus, and that more or less
vague and diffuse literary situation which preceded the great Neoplatonic
synthesis. Finally, as a relatively independent student of Pythagoreanism,
freely appropriating his sources for quite external purposes, Clement often
appears to be a good and disengaged testis.
What Did Clement Know about
Pythagoras and the Pythagorean Tradition?
Let us now turn to Clementâ€™s writings, looking everywhere for the Pythaï¿¾gorean elements in them.4 Clement speaks about Pythagoras in various
contexts and dedicates a special chapter (Stromateis V 27â€“30) to the Pythaï¿¾gorean symbolism. No surprise that for the lover of mysticism Pythagoras
was an ancient sage and religious reformer; a God-inspired transmitter of
the spiritual tradition, which itself reaches back to the most ancient times.
From the very beginning the Pythagorean School functioned as a secret
society and was shrouded in mystery.
Pythagoras from Samos,â€”says Clement,â€”was a son of Mnesarchus, as Hipï¿¾pobotus says. But Aristoxenus in his book the Life of Pythagoras, as well as
Aristarchus and Theopompus say that he came from Tyre, Neanthes from
Syria or Tyre, so the majority agrees that Pythagoras was of barbarian origin.
(Strom. I 62, 2â€“3; cf. Diog. Laert. VIII 1)
He was a student of Pherekydes5 and his floruit falls at the time of the dicï¿¾tatorship of Polycrates of Samos, around the sixty-second Olympiad [ci.
532â€“529bce].6 But the real teacher of his was certain Sonchis, the highest
prophet of the Egyptians.7 Pythagoras traveled a lot and even â€œâ€¦ underwent
circumcision in order to enter the Egyptian shrines to learn their philosoï¿¾phyâ€. He communicated with the best among the Chaldaeans and the Magi.

[L10] Introduction
[L11] Iamblichusâ€™ doctrine of the soul poses a problem of internal consistency.
[L12] Based on the fragments of De anima preserved in Joannes Stobaeusâ€™ writï¿¾ings, the evidence provided by Priscian of Lydia and the commentary on De
[L13] anima attributed to Simplicius in the manuscript tradition, the very core of
[L14] Iamblichusâ€™ doctrine would appear to coincide with the notion that once
[L15] the human soul has descended into the world of becoming and joined the
[L16] body, it changes in substance while preserving its identity.1 As it has been
[L17] emphasized in a number of important studies, this thesis was developed
[L18] and formulated by the philosopher in opposition to Plotinusâ€™ doctrine that
[L19] the soul possesses an immutable and unchanging nature. Usually referred
[L20] to as the doctrine of the â€œundescended soulâ€, the latter view rests on a strict
[L21] ontological distinction between the level of the soul and those of realities
[L22] superior to it.2 A closer examination of other fragments of De anima, as
[L23] well as evidence from Damascius and Hermias of Alexandria, would howï¿¾ever appear to challenge the above reconstruction of Iamblichusâ€™ doctrine.
[L24] Based on these sources, Iamblichus would seem to be drawing a hierarchiï¿¾cal distinction among individual souls according to their relation to change.
[L25] What he would be arguing is that the superior class of souls undergoes no
[L26] change in substance (see in particular Damascius, Commentary on Platoâ€™s
[L27] Parmenides IV p. 24.1â€“23 W.-C.). Iamblichus would thus appear to be grantï¿¾ing certain categories of souls the same status as the one Plotinus confers on
[L28] all soulsâ€”something Iamblichus criticises in other passages of his writing.
[L29] 1 See Prisc. Lyd. Metaphr. in Theophr. p. 32.13â€“19. See too J.F. Finamoreâ€”J.M. Dillon
[L30] (2002) 238â€“241, 258â€“259. Other crucial sources for Iamblichusâ€™ doctrine of the soul are Procl.
[L31] In Tim. iii p. 333. 23ff. and Simpl. (?) In de an. p. 5. 33ff., p. 89. 22ff., p. 240. 33ff., p. 313. 1ff.
[L32] (cf. ibid. p. 237. 37ff.). These passages have been collected and translated by A.J. FestugiÃ¨re
[L33] (1953) 252â€“257 and J. Finamore (2002) 229â€“278.

[L10] SH ï™‹ (Stobaeus, Anthology ï™„.ï™‡.ï™‹) comes from a chapter called â€œOn
[L11] <Divine> Necessity, by which things Planned by God Inevitably
[L12] Occur.â€ It is preceded by what appears below as SH ï™„ï™† (Anthology
[L13] ï™„.ï™‡.ï™Š
[L14] b

[L16] view is then complemented by the views of other famous philosophers
[L17] on the same topic (Anthology ï™„.ï™‡.ï™Šc
[L18] ). Jean-AndrÃ© FestugiÃ¨re underï¿¾stood SH ï™‹ to be part of the same treatise as SH ï™Š on the topic of
[L19] Providence, Necessity, and Fate.ï™„ Based strictly on its content, howï¿¾ever, SH ï™‹ might better be read (and indeed placed) among SH
[L20] ï™„ï™…â€“ï™„ï™‡.
[L21] After an introductory formula, Tat asks Hermes to distinguish the forces
[L22] of Providence, Necessity, and Fate. Hermes answers the question in a
[L23] roundabout way by distinguishing three kinds of bodiless entities separï¿¾ately governed by these three forces. Providence governs intellectual reality
[L24] (elsewhere called nous); Necessity governs non-rational forces (like drive
[L25] and desire), and Fate governs the incidental properties of bodies (qualities
[L26] like shape, color, place, and time).
[L27] Nous is free from the forces of Necessity and Fate when in direct
[L28] relation to God. When separated from God, nous falls into a body
[L29] according to the will of Providence. Nous is then joined to the nonï¿¾rational parts of the soul (namely, drive and desire) which are subject
[L30] to Necessity. Insofar as nous is joined with these lower parts of the
[L31] soul, it falls under the power of Necessity. When the human nous
[L32] turns toward God, however, it can both save the lower soul and
[L33] transcend the power of Necessity.
[L34] ï™„ NF ï™†.lxiâ€“lxii.

[L1] Finamore says:
[L2] For Neoplatonists, the vehicle fulfills three functions: it houses the rational
[L3] soul in its descent from the noetic realm to the realm of generation; it acts as
[L4] the organ of sense-perception and imagination; and, through theurgic rites,
[L5] it can be purified and lifted above, a vehicle for the rational soulâ€™s return
[L6] through the cosmos to the gods.10
[L7] Finamore outlines the Platonic sources of this doctrine: the Timaeus (41e)
[L8] where the Demiurge places souls in starry vehicles (ochÃªmata) and the Phaeï¿¾drus (247b) where the chariots of souls are, again, described as ochÃªmata.
[L9] Perhaps more significantly, as regards physical sensation, is Aristotleâ€™s theï¿¾ory (De Gen. An. 736b)â€”also noted by Finamoreâ€”that each soul has a
[L10] pneumatic body made like the heavenly aether to serve as intermediary
[L11] between the immaterial soul and the physical senses. As Finamore puts it:
[L12] â€œâ€¦ it is a small step for later philosophers to combine Aristotleâ€™s pneuma
[L13] with ether, the element of the stars, and with the â€˜Platonicâ€™ ochÃªma onto
[L14] which the Demiurge placed the soul.â€11 Thus, for Iamblichus, souls have an
[L15] eternal aetheric vehicle that animates the body with breath (pneuma) and
[L16] coordinates sense impressions. The soulâ€™s vehicle is also associated with
[L17] phantasia, and this follows Aristotleâ€™s belief that mental images are necesï¿¾sary for engaging the world. The imagination, like the ochÃªma itself, funcï¿¾tions as a kind of intermediary between material and immaterial realms.
[L18] If it is through the ochÃªma that the soul becomes a god, it necessarily
[L19] plays a critical role in theurgic divination. Fortunately, Iamblichus provides
[L20] quite a few clues about how this transformation happens, but it is easy
[L21] to misunderstand what he says. As scholars we are far more the heirs of
[L22] Porphyry than of Iamblichus, and to grasp the theurgic function of the
[L23] ochÃªma we naturally look for discursively coherent descriptions. Porphyry
[L24] did just that. He asked Iamblichus for a diarthrÃ´sis, a precise articulation
[L25] of the divination of the futureâ€”a request which seems reasonable to usâ€”
[L26] but Iamblichus objects to the question and chastises Porphyry for assuming
[L27] that divination could be understood as if it were a natural phenomenon or
[L28] human technique capable of discursive analysis.12 He says:
[L29] 10 J. Finamore (1985) 1.
[L30] 11 Ibid., 2.
[L31] 12 DM 99.10â€“100.6; diarthrÃ´thÃªnai at 99.9.<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 106 / 212>the role of aesthesis in theurgy 95
[L32] For, according to the gist of your question, you believe something like this
[L33] about foreknowledge: â€œthat it can come into being,â€ and is among â€œthe things

[L35] it does not behave at all like a natural change, nor is it an artifact invented for
[L36] use in daily life, nor is it, generally, a human achievement at all.

[L1] Human bodies can travel only so far as their
[L2] nature and the astral powers allow (Â§ï™‡ï™‹â€“ï™‡ï™Œ).
[L3] In preparation for human embodiment, a second creation story is told
[L4] (Â§Â§ï™ˆï™ƒâ€“ï™ˆï™…). According to this account, God calls a (second) council of star
[L5] gods. As they once provided their gifts to humankind, so now they offer
[L6] their products to the world. The mist and dark chaos that is lower earth is
[L7] immediately ordered and made visible to the heavens above. God himself
[L8] directly provides all the products of nature to the world system. The earth
[L9] is ready to receive the host of souls.
[L10] Locked up in mortal bodies, the souls prove utterly rebellious and
[L11] barbarous. Remembering their noble birth, humans impiously assault the
[L12] star gods. On earth, the strong ruthlessly oppress the weak. Temples are
[L13] polluted with the bodies of the butchered. There are no laws and no fear of
[L14] punishment (Â§Â§ï™ˆï™†â€“ï™ˆï™‡).
[L15] Four figures rise to call a halt to human savagery. They are the elements:
[L16] Fire, Air, Water, and Earth. Lamenting their defilement, the elements
[L17] petition the supreme God to send a subordinate god to civilize the world
[L18] (Â§Â§ï™ˆï™ˆâ€“ï™‰ï™„). The high God responds by sending his own emanation in the
[L19] form of Isis and Osiris. When they arrive on earth, they fill it with
[L20] the blessings of law, art, and religious rites. They discover and ensure the
[L21] preservation of Hermesâ€™s ancient wisdom. After prayer, Isis and Osiris
[L22] re-ascend â€“ like Hermes of old â€“ to their heavenly home.
[L23] With their return journey complete, the tractate comes to a close. Isis
[L24] herself guarantees that true knowledge of creation will persist on earth by
[L25] passing it on to her son Horus â€“ and hence to all future readers of
[L26] Hermetic lore.
[L27] What the Hermetic reader must remember is that despite their savagery
[L28] and capacity to degrade, humans are essentially divine. They come from
[L29] the realm of the stars. Their souls are of varying quality, but none of them
[L30] lacks divinity. All they need is a guide to bring them the culture and
[L31] education that will allow them to remember who they are.
[L32] Earlier interpreters who championed the influence of Jewish myth (in
[L33] particular, the fallen angel myth of ï™„ Enoch) emphasized the theme of
[L34] forbidden knowledge.ï™„ï™„ Yet knowledge is never actually forbidden in the
[L35] KorÄ“ Kosmou. It is God who invites the lower world to search for and find
[L36] him. God wills to bring order out of disorder, and the creation of souls is
[L37] ï™„ï™„ See the summary of Ferguson, Hermetica, ï™‡.xxxivâ€“xlii, who discusses Wilhelm Bousset and other
[L38] earlier interpreters.

[L190] Preface
[L191] Despite widespread interest in the Hermetica across the globe, currently
[L192] there does not exist a reliable and up-to-date English translation of the
[L193] various Hermetic fragments and testimonies. Indeed, some of these fragï¿¾ments and testimonies remain generally unknown.
[L194] At the turn of the twentieth century, G. R. S. Mead made a translation
[L195] of select fragments into Victorian English from now-outdated editions.
[L196] The translation of the fragments by Walter Scott (ï™„ï™Œï™…ï™‡) was based on his
[L197] own re-written Greek text â€“ a jungle of emendations and transpositions.
[L198] When it came to testimonies, moreover, Scott did not actually translate the
[L199] Greek or Latin texts. He only rendered into English (via Latin translations)
[L200] texts originally written in Arabic.
[L201] The present translation serves a new generation of scholarly and lay
[L202] readers of the Hermetica. It remains faithful to â€“ though critically engaged
[L203] with â€“ the various manuscript traditions. Copious notes provide historical
[L204] background, parallels, and references for further reading. Included also are
[L205] many testimonies that Scott did not print. It is hoped that this volume will
[L206] spark interest in the full reception history of the Hermetica, which must
[L207] include Late Antiquity and the medieval period.
[L208] Here I gratefully acknowledge persons who read parts of the manuscript
[L209] and offered helpful suggestions: Brian P. Copenhaver, David Runia, and
[L210] Christian H. Bull. Christian Wildberg and Kevin Van Bladel also kindly
[L211] answered my inquiries and provided guidance based on their expertise. My
[L212] thanks also to Oxford University Press for the use of Van Bladelâ€™s translaï¿¾tions of Arabic source materials.

[L299] General Introduction
[L300] There are five groups of philosophical Hermetic writings that do not
[L301] appear in Brian P. Copenhaverâ€™s ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™… English translation entitled Hermeï¿¾tica: The Greek â€œCorpus Hermeticumâ€ and the Latin â€œAsclepiusâ€.
[L302] ï™„ The first is
[L303] the large group of Hermetic excerpts from Stobaeus, an early fifth-century
[L304] ï£ï¥ anthologist. The second is the Coptic Hermetica (discovered in ï™„ï™Œï™‡ï™ˆ)
[L305] featuring two excerpts of previously known writings in addition to a
[L306] formerly unknown Hermetic tractate (the Discourse on the Eighth and
[L307] Ninth). The third group is the collection of Hermetic Definitions, a set
[L308] of maxims extant in Greek fragments but preserved only fully in Armeï¿¾nian. The fourth is the previously known set of diverse fragments quoted
[L309] by various (largely Christian) authors in Late Antiquity. The fifth comï¿¾prises recently discovered Hermetic fragments currently preserved in
[L310] Vienna and Oxford.
[L311] The Coptic Hermetica are widely available in English translations of the
[L312] Nag Hammadi library.ï™… The Definitions are now conveniently accessible in
[L313] English thanks to the work of Jean-Pierre MahÃ©.ï™† What remains to be
[L314] translated are the fragments from Stobaeus, the fragments and testimonies
[L315] from various authors, and the fragments from the newly discovered papyri.
[L316] ï™„ Copenhaverâ€™s introduction to CH and Ascl. remain relevant (Copenhaver, xxxiiâ€“xlv). See also Peter
[L317] Kingsley, â€œAn Introduction to the Hermetica: Approaching Ancient Esoteric Tradition,â€ in Roelof
[L318] van den Broek and Cis van Heertum, eds., From Poimandres to Jacob BÃ¶hme: Gnosis: Hermetism and
[L319] the Christian Tradition (Amsterdam: In de Pelikaan, ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒï™ƒ), ï™„ï™Šâ€“ï™‡ï™ƒ. ï™… See Marvin Meyer, ed., Nag Hammadi Scriptures: The International Edition (New York: HarperOne,
[L320] ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒï™‹), ï™‡ï™ƒï™Œâ€“ï™†ï™‰. Introductions to the Coptic Hermetica can be found in ibid., ï™‡ï™ƒï™Œâ€“ï™„ï™…; ï™‡ï™„ï™Œâ€“ï™…ï™„;
[L321] ï™‡ï™…ï™ˆâ€“ï™…ï™Œ; MahÃ©, HHE, ï™„.ï™†ï™„â€“ï™ˆï™„, ï™„ï™†ï™Šâ€“ï™ˆï™ˆ; ï™….ï™‡ï™Šâ€“ï™„ï™‡ï™‡; Hans-Martin Schenke, Hans-Gebhard Bethge,
