[L334] It is high time to present a new translation and annotation of these (chiefly
[L335] philosophical) Hermetica to the English-speaking world.
[L336] Hermes-Thoth
[L337] Nothing binds together the multifarious Hermetic fragments beyond their
[L338] ascription to Hermes Thrice Great. Hermes Thrice Great is a fictional
[L339] character. Yet for many in the ancient world that fiction was history. If we
[L340] call Hermes Thrice Great a â€œmyth,â€ we thereby recognize that he is greater
[L341] and more significant than any one historical figure. How do we introduce a
[L342] figure that appears in so many different ages in so many different guises? If
[L343] there was an â€œoriginalâ€ Hermes Thrice Great, we are obliged to pick up the
[L344] thread at significant points of reception.
[L345] Iamblichus (about ï™…ï™‡ï™ˆâ€“ï™†ï™…ï™ˆ ï£ï¥) commences his book (later called On
[L346] the Mysteries) with the following flourish:
[L347] Hermes, the deity who presides over rational discourses, has long and
[L348] rightly been considered common to all who practice the sacred arts. He
[L349] who presides over true science concerning gods is one and the same
[L350] throughout the universe. It is to him that our ancestors dedicated the
[L351] discoveries of their wisdom, attributing all their own writings to Hermes.ï™‡
[L352] Important here is the frank acknowledgement that many authors wrote
[L353] under the name of Hermes. The practice of pseudepigraphy was logical for
[L354] devotees of Hermes.ï™ˆ True wisdom and learning merited ascription to the
[L355] lord of all learning. This is why many Egyptian scholars attributed their
[L356] writings to Hermes. Iamblichus, himself writing under a false name
[L357] (â€œAbammon,â€ an Egyptian priest), calls these writers his â€œancestors.â€ These
[L358] â€œancestorsâ€ were probably Hellenized Egyptian scribes and priests who
[L359] lived not very long before Iamblichus himself.
[L360] Later, Iamblichus gives a taste of how many persons were writing under
[L361] the name of Hermes. In On the Mysteries ï™‹.ï™„, he passes on the report of a
[L362] certain Seleucus, who attributed to Hermes a total of ï™…ï™ƒ,ï™ƒï™ƒï™ƒ books.
[L363] A better-known source, the Egyptian priest and historian Manetho, nearly

[L388] The many genres of Hermetic learning are well illustrated by a passage

[L398] for kings. As a supplement, six books on medical matters (anatomy,
[L399] medicines, medical instruments, and gynecology) were displayed.ï™Œ All this
[L400] vast store of knowledge was ascribed to Hermes.

[L420] helmeted with the cap of invisibility, wielding the twisted caduceus with

[L422] Egyptian Hermes, on the other hand, was Thoth, depicted as the ibisï¿¾headed scribe of the gods, secretary of Re, giver of oracles, master of magic,
[L423] lord of the moon often appearing in the form of a dog-faced baboon.
[L424] When the Greeks dominated Egypt, they identified the Egyptian god
[L425] Thoth with Hermes. But why? What did they see in these two gods that
[L426] was similar?
[L427] There are several overlaps, yet we will focus on two.ï™„ï™„ First of all,
[L428] Hermes as psychopomp, or escort of the dead, resembled Thoth as seen in
[L429] the various versions of the Egyptian Book of the Dead (or the Book of Going
[L430] Forth by Day), chapter ï™„ï™…ï™ˆ. In the Hall of Two Truths, Thoth hears the
[L431] confession of the deceased person. After the personâ€™s heart is weighed
[L432] against the feather of Maat (â€œJustice,â€ or â€œTruthâ€), Thoth carefully
[L433] inscribes the result with his tablet and stylus. If the heart is pure, Thoth
[L434] leads the candidate into the presence of Osiris, the ultimate judge of the
[L435] dead, and finally into the Field of Reeds.ï™„ï™… Perhaps the most famous scene
[L436] of Hermes leading souls is the opening of Homerâ€™s Odyssey, book ï™…ï™‡. In
[L437] this scene, Hermes guides the freshly slaughtered suitors of Penelope to the
[L438] netherworld. Mindless, the suitors squeak like bats on their way to the
[L439] halls of Hades.
[L440] Yet there was a more basic similarity between Hermes and Thoth.
[L441] Hermes is more than a herald or messenger. He is the Logos â€“ the Reason,
[L442] Speech, or Word of God.ï™„ï™† The Word devises speech and brings his own
[L443] interpretation, which the Greeks called hermeneia.
[L444] ï™„ï™‡ Thoth is also the god
[L445] who presides over speech and interpretation. He is called â€œthe heart of Re,
[L446] the tongue of Atum, the throat of the God whose name is hidden.â€ As
[L447] ï™„ï™ƒ The â€œwinged son of kindly Maiaâ€ derives from Horace, Odes, ï™„.ï™†. ï™„ï™„ Other similarities between Thoth and Hermes are catalogued by Maria-Theresia Derchain-Urtel,

[L472] Greeks typically conceived of Hermes Thrice Great in a Euhemeristic
[L473] fashion. That is to say, they often considered him to have been an ancient
[L474] man â€“ a real scribe of a real Pharaoh, often the first divine Pharaoh called
[L475] Ammon (the Egyptian god Amun). This scribe, named Thoth or Theuth,
[L476] invented the alphabet and the art of writing.ï™„ï™‰ Ever since, humans have
[L477] been using writing to preserve the vast array of accumulated knowledge.
[L478] Thoth was later deified to become a recognized Egyptian god or daimon (a
[L479] kind of mediating deity).ï™„ï™Š Writing was the best-known benefit that
[L480] Thoth offered to human beings, but it was not the only one.

[L10] Julian, Al-KindÄ«, Michael Psellus, the Emerald Tablet, and Nicholas

[L13] appeal to a broad array of readers interested in western esotericism

[L189] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 11 / 272>
[L190] Preface
[L191] Despite widespread interest in the Hermetica across the globe, currently
[L192] there does not exist a reliable and up-to-date English translation of the
[L193] various Hermetic fragments and testimonies. Indeed, some of these fragï¿¾ments and testimonies remain generally unknown.
[L194] At the turn of the twentieth century, G. R. S. Mead made a translation
[L195] of select fragments into Victorian English from now-outdated editions.
[L196] The translation of the fragments by Walter Scott (ï™„ï™Œï™…ï™‡) was based on his
[L197] own re-written Greek text â€“ a jungle of emendations and transpositions.
[L198] When it came to testimonies, moreover, Scott did not actually translate the
[L199] Greek or Latin texts. He only rendered into English (via Latin translations)
[L200] texts originally written in Arabic.
[L201] The present translation serves a new generation of scholarly and lay
[L202] readers of the Hermetica. It remains faithful to â€“ though critically engaged
[L203] with â€“ the various manuscript traditions. Copious notes provide historical
[L204] background, parallels, and references for further reading. Included also are
[L205] many testimonies that Scott did not print. It is hoped that this volume will
[L206] spark interest in the full reception history of the Hermetica, which must
[L207] include Late Antiquity and the medieval period.
[L208] Here I gratefully acknowledge persons who read parts of the manuscript
[L209] and offered helpful suggestions: Brian P. Copenhaver, David Runia, and
[L210] Christian H. Bull. Christian Wildberg and Kevin Van Bladel also kindly
[L211] answered my inquiries and provided guidance based on their expertise. My
[L212] thanks also to Oxford University Press for the use of Van Bladelâ€™s translaï¿¾tions of Arabic source materials.

[L299] General Introduction
[L300] There are five groups of philosophical Hermetic writings that do not
[L301] appear in Brian P. Copenhaverâ€™s ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™… English translation entitled Hermeï¿¾tica: The Greek â€œCorpus Hermeticumâ€ and the Latin â€œAsclepiusâ€.
[L302] ï™„ The first is
[L303] the large group of Hermetic excerpts from Stobaeus, an early fifth-century
[L304] ï£ï¥ anthologist. The second is the Coptic Hermetica (discovered in ï™„ï™Œï™‡ï™ˆ)
[L305] featuring two excerpts of previously known writings in addition to a
[L306] formerly unknown Hermetic tractate (the Discourse on the Eighth and
[L307] Ninth). The third group is the collection of Hermetic Definitions, a set
[L308] of maxims extant in Greek fragments but preserved only fully in Armeï¿¾nian. The fourth is the previously known set of diverse fragments quoted
[L309] by various (largely Christian) authors in Late Antiquity. The fifth comï¿¾prises recently discovered Hermetic fragments currently preserved in
[L310] Vienna and Oxford.
[L311] The Coptic Hermetica are widely available in English translations of the
[L312] Nag Hammadi library.ï™… The Definitions are now conveniently accessible in
[L313] English thanks to the work of Jean-Pierre MahÃ©.ï™† What remains to be
[L314] translated are the fragments from Stobaeus, the fragments and testimonies
[L315] from various authors, and the fragments from the newly discovered papyri.

[L334] It is high time to present a new translation and annotation of these (chiefly
[L335] philosophical) Hermetica to the English-speaking world.
[L336] Hermes-Thoth
[L337] Nothing binds together the multifarious Hermetic fragments beyond their
[L338] ascription to Hermes Thrice Great. Hermes Thrice Great is a fictional
[L339] character. Yet for many in the ancient world that fiction was history. If we
[L340] call Hermes Thrice Great a â€œmyth,â€ we thereby recognize that he is greater
[L341] and more significant than any one historical figure. How do we introduce a
[L342] figure that appears in so many different ages in so many different guises? If
[L343] there was an â€œoriginalâ€ Hermes Thrice Great, we are obliged to pick up the
[L344] thread at significant points of reception.
[L345] Iamblichus (about ï™…ï™‡ï™ˆâ€“ï™†ï™…ï™ˆ ï£ï¥) commences his book (later called On
[L346] the Mysteries) with the following flourish:
[L347] Hermes, the deity who presides over rational discourses, has long and
[L348] rightly been considered common to all who practice the sacred arts. He
[L349] who presides over true science concerning gods is one and the same
[L350] throughout the universe. It is to him that our ancestors dedicated the
[L351] discoveries of their wisdom, attributing all their own writings to Hermes.ï™‡
[L352] Important here is the frank acknowledgement that many authors wrote
[L353] under the name of Hermes. The practice of pseudepigraphy was logical for
[L354] devotees of Hermes.ï™ˆ True wisdom and learning merited ascription to the
[L355] lord of all learning. This is why many Egyptian scholars attributed their
[L356] writings to Hermes. Iamblichus, himself writing under a false name
[L357] (â€œAbammon,â€ an Egyptian priest), calls these writers his â€œancestors.â€ These
[L358] â€œancestorsâ€ were probably Hellenized Egyptian scribes and priests who
[L359] lived not very long before Iamblichus himself.
[L360] Later, Iamblichus gives a taste of how many persons were writing under
[L361] the name of Hermes. In On the Mysteries ï™‹.ï™„, he passes on the report of a
[L362] certain Seleucus, who attributed to Hermes a total of ï™…ï™ƒ,ï™ƒï™ƒï™ƒ books.
[L363] A better-known source, the Egyptian priest and historian Manetho, nearly
[L364] ï™‡ Iamblichus, On the Mysteries, ï™„.ï™„. ï™ˆ On Hermetic pseudepigraphy, see Anthony Grafton, Forgers and Critics: Creativity and Duplicity in
[L365] Western Scholarship (Princeton: Princeton University Press, ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™ƒ), ï™‹ï™ˆâ€“ï™Œï™‹; and more generally, Bart
[L366] D. Ehrman, Forgery and Counterforgery: The Use of Literary Deceit in Early Christian Polemics

[L375] doubled this number, crediting Hermes with ï™†ï™‰,ï™ˆï™…ï™ˆ volumes (or
[L376] ï™„ï™ƒï™ƒ volumes for each single day in a ï™‡-year period).ï™‰ So many books
[L377] would easily fill the shelves of a large temple library (the Egyptian â€œHouse
[L378] of Lifeâ€). Perhaps this is the point â€“ all the wisdom of the Egyptian sacred

[L380] According to Iamblichus, Hermes wrote hundreds of tomes on specialï¿¾ized topics like â€œthe gods in the fiery zone,â€ â€œthe gods in the aether,â€ and
[L381] â€œthe gods in the heavens.â€
[L382] ï™Š Hermes was the ultimate theologian, yet the
[L383] scope of his expertise was in fact more vast. There are existing treatises on
[L384] astrology, the virtues of stones, the principles of creation, the origin and
[L385] nature of the soul, alchemical practices, Fate, the effects of climate on
[L386] intelligence, healing, and even why children resemble their parents â€“ all
[L387] ascribed to Hermes.ï™‹
[L388] The many genres of Hermetic learning are well illustrated by a passage
[L389] in Clement of Alexandria. This Christian writer around ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒ ï£ï¥ describes a
[L390] procession of Egyptian officials in which forty-two fundamental writings of
[L391] Hermes were displayed (the number of Egyptâ€™s districts or â€œnomesâ€). The
[L392] highest-ranking priest, whom the Greeks called â€œProphet,â€ carried the ten
[L393] â€œhieraticâ€ books on laws, the gods, and the training of priests. The Stoleï¿¾keeper presented ten books on education and sacrifice. The Sacred Scribe
[L394] held up a decade of books on hieroglyphs, geography, and the temples.
[L395] Then came the Astrologer, who showed four books on astronomical
[L396] matters (fixed stars, planets, conjunctions, and the risings of astral bodies).
[L397] Finally, the Singer held in his hands a songbook and an instruction manual
[L398] for kings. As a supplement, six books on medical matters (anatomy,
[L399] medicines, medical instruments, and gynecology) were displayed.ï™Œ All this
[L400] vast store of knowledge was ascribed to Hermes.
[L401] Who was this Hermes? We must first of all distinguish a Greek deity
[L402] from a significantly different Egyptian one. The Greek Hermes was the
[L403] â€œwinged son of kindly Maia,â€ racing on the winds as Zeusâ€™s crafty herald,

[L420] helmeted with the cap of invisibility, wielding the twisted caduceus with
[L421] the power to put even Argus with his hundred eyes to sleep.ï™„ï™ƒ The
[L422] Egyptian Hermes, on the other hand, was Thoth, depicted as the ibisï¿¾headed scribe of the gods, secretary of Re, giver of oracles, master of magic,
[L423] lord of the moon often appearing in the form of a dog-faced baboon.
[L424] When the Greeks dominated Egypt, they identified the Egyptian god
[L425] Thoth with Hermes. But why? What did they see in these two gods that
[L426] was similar?
[L427] There are several overlaps, yet we will focus on two.ï™„ï™„ First of all,
[L428] Hermes as psychopomp, or escort of the dead, resembled Thoth as seen in
[L429] the various versions of the Egyptian Book of the Dead (or the Book of Going
[L430] Forth by Day), chapter ï™„ï™…ï™ˆ. In the Hall of Two Truths, Thoth hears the
[L431] confession of the deceased person. After the personâ€™s heart is weighed
[L432] against the feather of Maat (â€œJustice,â€ or â€œTruthâ€), Thoth carefully
[L433] inscribes the result with his tablet and stylus. If the heart is pure, Thoth
[L434] leads the candidate into the presence of Osiris, the ultimate judge of the
[L435] dead, and finally into the Field of Reeds.ï™„ï™… Perhaps the most famous scene
[L436] of Hermes leading souls is the opening of Homerâ€™s Odyssey, book ï™…ï™‡. In
[L437] this scene, Hermes guides the freshly slaughtered suitors of Penelope to the
[L438] netherworld. Mindless, the suitors squeak like bats on their way to the
[L439] halls of Hades.
[L440] Yet there was a more basic similarity between Hermes and Thoth.
[L441] Hermes is more than a herald or messenger. He is the Logos â€“ the Reason,
[L442] Speech, or Word of God.ï™„ï™† The Word devises speech and brings his own
[L443] interpretation, which the Greeks called hermeneia.
[L444] ï™„ï™‡ Thoth is also the god
[L445] who presides over speech and interpretation. He is called â€œthe heart of Re,
[L446] the tongue of Atum, the throat of the God whose name is hidden.â€ As
[L447] ï™„ï™ƒ The â€œwinged son of kindly Maiaâ€ derives from Horace, Odes, ï™„.ï™†. ï™„ï™„ Other similarities between Thoth and Hermes are catalogued by Maria-Theresia Derchain-Urtel,

[L467] In the Hellenistic period (roughly ï™†ï™…ï™†â€“ï™†ï™„ ï¢ï£ï¥), Greeks living in Egypt
[L468] and Hellenized Egyptians crafted an amalgam of Thoth and Hermes who
[L469] was called â€œthe Egyptian Hermesâ€ or later â€œHermes Thrice Greatestâ€
[L470] (Trismegistos, Termaximus). This Hermes is superlatively great in a superlaï¿¾tive (threefold) way. Following English convention, however, here we call
[L471] â€œThrice Greatest Hermesâ€ simply â€œHermes Thrice Great.â€
[L472] Greeks typically conceived of Hermes Thrice Great in a Euhemeristic
[L473] fashion. That is to say, they often considered him to have been an ancient
[L474] man â€“ a real scribe of a real Pharaoh, often the first divine Pharaoh called
[L475] Ammon (the Egyptian god Amun). This scribe, named Thoth or Theuth,
[L476] invented the alphabet and the art of writing.ï™„ï™‰ Ever since, humans have
[L477] been using writing to preserve the vast array of accumulated knowledge.
[L478] Thoth was later deified to become a recognized Egyptian god or daimon (a
[L479] kind of mediating deity).ï™„ï™Š Writing was the best-known benefit that
[L480] Thoth offered to human beings, but it was not the only one.
[L481] Greeks attributed to Thoth the invention of a host of other arts. Plato
[L482] (ï™‡ï™…ï™Šâ€“ï™†ï™‡ï™Š ï¢ï£ï¥) made Thoth the discoverer of mathematics and astronï¿¾omy.
[L483] ï™„ï™‹ Hecataeus of Abdera (fourth century ï¢ï£ï¥) ascribed to him the
[L484] invention of a common language, religious ritual, music, wrestling,
[L485] dancing, and the culture of the olive.ï™„ï™Œ The Jewish writer Artapanus (third

[L2] HERMETICA II
[L3] This volume presents in new English translations the scattered fragï¿¾ments and testimonies regarding Hermes Thrice Great that complete
[L4] Brian Copenhaverâ€™s translation of the Hermetica (Cambridge ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™…).
[L5] It contains the twenty-nine fragments from Stobaeus (including the
[L6] famous KorÄ“ Kosmou), the Oxford and Vienna fragments (never
[L7] before translated into English), an expanded selection of fragments
[L8] from various authors (including Zosimus of Panopolis, Augustine,
[L9] and Albert the Great), and testimonies about Hermes from thirtyï¿¾eight authors (including Cicero, Pseudo-Manetho, the Emperor
[L10] Julian, Al-KindÄ«, Michael Psellus, the Emerald Tablet, and Nicholas
[L11] of Cusa). All translations are accompanied by introductions and notes
[L12] which cite sources for further reading. These Hermetic texts will
[L13] appeal to a broad array of readers interested in western esotericism
[L14] including scholars of Egyptology, the New Testament, the classical
[L15] world, Byzantium, medieval Islam, the Latin Middle Ages, and the
[L16] Renaissance.

[L189] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 11 / 272>
[L190] Preface
[L191] Despite widespread interest in the Hermetica across the globe, currently
[L192] there does not exist a reliable and up-to-date English translation of the
[L193] various Hermetic fragments and testimonies. Indeed, some of these fragï¿¾ments and testimonies remain generally unknown.
[L194] At the turn of the twentieth century, G. R. S. Mead made a translation
[L195] of select fragments into Victorian English from now-outdated editions.
[L196] The translation of the fragments by Walter Scott (ï™„ï™Œï™…ï™‡) was based on his
[L197] own re-written Greek text â€“ a jungle of emendations and transpositions.
[L198] When it came to testimonies, moreover, Scott did not actually translate the
[L199] Greek or Latin texts. He only rendered into English (via Latin translations)
[L200] texts originally written in Arabic.
[L201] The present translation serves a new generation of scholarly and lay
[L202] readers of the Hermetica. It remains faithful to â€“ though critically engaged
[L203] with â€“ the various manuscript traditions. Copious notes provide historical
[L204] background, parallels, and references for further reading. Included also are
[L205] many testimonies that Scott did not print. It is hoped that this volume will
[L206] spark interest in the full reception history of the Hermetica, which must
[L207] include Late Antiquity and the medieval period.
[L208] Here I gratefully acknowledge persons who read parts of the manuscript

[L299] General Introduction
[L300] There are five groups of philosophical Hermetic writings that do not
[L301] appear in Brian P. Copenhaverâ€™s ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™… English translation entitled Hermeï¿¾tica: The Greek â€œCorpus Hermeticumâ€ and the Latin â€œAsclepiusâ€.
[L302] ï™„ The first is
[L303] the large group of Hermetic excerpts from Stobaeus, an early fifth-century
[L304] ï£ï¥ anthologist. The second is the Coptic Hermetica (discovered in ï™„ï™Œï™‡ï™ˆ)
[L305] featuring two excerpts of previously known writings in addition to a
[L306] formerly unknown Hermetic tractate (the Discourse on the Eighth and
[L307] Ninth). The third group is the collection of Hermetic Definitions, a set
[L308] of maxims extant in Greek fragments but preserved only fully in Armeï¿¾nian. The fourth is the previously known set of diverse fragments quoted
[L309] by various (largely Christian) authors in Late Antiquity. The fifth comï¿¾prises recently discovered Hermetic fragments currently preserved in
[L310] Vienna and Oxford.
[L311] The Coptic Hermetica are widely available in English translations of the
[L312] Nag Hammadi library.ï™… The Definitions are now conveniently accessible in
[L313] English thanks to the work of Jean-Pierre MahÃ©.ï™† What remains to be
[L314] translated are the fragments from Stobaeus, the fragments and testimonies
[L315] from various authors, and the fragments from the newly discovered papyri.

[L334] It is high time to present a new translation and annotation of these (chiefly
[L335] philosophical) Hermetica to the English-speaking world.
[L336] Hermes-Thoth
[L337] Nothing binds together the multifarious Hermetic fragments beyond their
[L338] ascription to Hermes Thrice Great. Hermes Thrice Great is a fictional
[L339] character. Yet for many in the ancient world that fiction was history. If we
[L340] call Hermes Thrice Great a â€œmyth,â€ we thereby recognize that he is greater
[L341] and more significant than any one historical figure. How do we introduce a
[L342] figure that appears in so many different ages in so many different guises? If
[L343] there was an â€œoriginalâ€ Hermes Thrice Great, we are obliged to pick up the
[L344] thread at significant points of reception.
[L345] Iamblichus (about ï™…ï™‡ï™ˆâ€“ï™†ï™…ï™ˆ ï£ï¥) commences his book (later called On
[L346] the Mysteries) with the following flourish:
[L347] Hermes, the deity who presides over rational discourses, has long and
[L348] rightly been considered common to all who practice the sacred arts. He
[L349] who presides over true science concerning gods is one and the same
[L350] throughout the universe. It is to him that our ancestors dedicated the
[L351] discoveries of their wisdom, attributing all their own writings to Hermes.ï™‡
[L352] Important here is the frank acknowledgement that many authors wrote
[L353] under the name of Hermes. The practice of pseudepigraphy was logical for
[L354] devotees of Hermes.ï™ˆ True wisdom and learning merited ascription to the
[L355] lord of all learning. This is why many Egyptian scholars attributed their
[L356] writings to Hermes. Iamblichus, himself writing under a false name
[L357] (â€œAbammon,â€ an Egyptian priest), calls these writers his â€œancestors.â€ These
[L358] â€œancestorsâ€ were probably Hellenized Egyptian scribes and priests who
[L359] lived not very long before Iamblichus himself.
[L360] Later, Iamblichus gives a taste of how many persons were writing under
[L361] the name of Hermes. In On the Mysteries ï™‹.ï™„, he passes on the report of a
[L362] certain Seleucus, who attributed to Hermes a total of ï™…ï™ƒ,ï™ƒï™ƒï™ƒ books.
[L363] A better-known source, the Egyptian priest and historian Manetho, nearly
[L364] ï™‡ Iamblichus, On the Mysteries, ï™„.ï™„. ï™ˆ On Hermetic pseudepigraphy, see Anthony Grafton, Forgers and Critics: Creativity and Duplicity in
[L365] Western Scholarship (Princeton: Princeton University Press, ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™ƒ), ï™‹ï™ˆâ€“ï™Œï™‹; and more generally, Bart
[L366] D. Ehrman, Forgery and Counterforgery: The Use of Literary Deceit in Early Christian Polemics
[L367] (Oxford: Oxford University Press, ï™…ï™ƒï™„ï™†), ï™„ï™„â€“ï™„ï™‡ï™ˆ; ï™ˆï™†ï™‡â€“ï™‡ï™‹.

[L375] doubled this number, crediting Hermes with ï™†ï™‰,ï™ˆï™…ï™ˆ volumes (or
[L376] ï™„ï™ƒï™ƒ volumes for each single day in a ï™‡-year period).ï™‰ So many books
[L377] would easily fill the shelves of a large temple library (the Egyptian â€œHouse
[L378] of Lifeâ€). Perhaps this is the point â€“ all the wisdom of the Egyptian sacred
[L379] priesthood ultimately reverts to Hermes.
[L380] According to Iamblichus, Hermes wrote hundreds of tomes on specialï¿¾ized topics like â€œthe gods in the fiery zone,â€ â€œthe gods in the aether,â€ and
[L381] â€œthe gods in the heavens.â€
[L382] ï™Š Hermes was the ultimate theologian, yet the
[L383] scope of his expertise was in fact more vast. There are existing treatises on
[L384] astrology, the virtues of stones, the principles of creation, the origin and
[L385] nature of the soul, alchemical practices, Fate, the effects of climate on
[L386] intelligence, healing, and even why children resemble their parents â€“ all
[L387] ascribed to Hermes.ï™‹
[L388] The many genres of Hermetic learning are well illustrated by a passage
[L389] in Clement of Alexandria. This Christian writer around ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒ ï£ï¥ describes a
[L390] procession of Egyptian officials in which forty-two fundamental writings of
[L391] Hermes were displayed (the number of Egyptâ€™s districts or â€œnomesâ€). The
[L392] highest-ranking priest, whom the Greeks called â€œProphet,â€ carried the ten
[L393] â€œhieraticâ€ books on laws, the gods, and the training of priests. The Stoleï¿¾keeper presented ten books on education and sacrifice. The Sacred Scribe
[L394] held up a decade of books on hieroglyphs, geography, and the temples.
[L395] Then came the Astrologer, who showed four books on astronomical
[L396] matters (fixed stars, planets, conjunctions, and the risings of astral bodies).
[L397] Finally, the Singer held in his hands a songbook and an instruction manual
[L398] for kings. As a supplement, six books on medical matters (anatomy,
[L399] medicines, medical instruments, and gynecology) were displayed.ï™Œ All this
[L400] vast store of knowledge was ascribed to Hermes.
[L401] Who was this Hermes? We must first of all distinguish a Greek deity
[L402] from a significantly different Egyptian one. The Greek Hermes was the
[L403] â€œwinged son of kindly Maia,â€ racing on the winds as Zeusâ€™s crafty herald,

[L420] helmeted with the cap of invisibility, wielding the twisted caduceus with
[L421] the power to put even Argus with his hundred eyes to sleep.ï™„ï™ƒ The
[L422] Egyptian Hermes, on the other hand, was Thoth, depicted as the ibisï¿¾headed scribe of the gods, secretary of Re, giver of oracles, master of magic,
[L423] lord of the moon often appearing in the form of a dog-faced baboon.
[L424] When the Greeks dominated Egypt, they identified the Egyptian god
[L425] Thoth with Hermes. But why? What did they see in these two gods that
[L426] was similar?
[L427] There are several overlaps, yet we will focus on two.ï™„ï™„ First of all,
[L428] Hermes as psychopomp, or escort of the dead, resembled Thoth as seen in
[L429] the various versions of the Egyptian Book of the Dead (or the Book of Going
[L430] Forth by Day), chapter ï™„ï™…ï™ˆ. In the Hall of Two Truths, Thoth hears the
[L431] confession of the deceased person. After the personâ€™s heart is weighed
[L432] against the feather of Maat (â€œJustice,â€ or â€œTruthâ€), Thoth carefully
[L433] inscribes the result with his tablet and stylus. If the heart is pure, Thoth
[L434] leads the candidate into the presence of Osiris, the ultimate judge of the
[L435] dead, and finally into the Field of Reeds.ï™„ï™… Perhaps the most famous scene
[L436] of Hermes leading souls is the opening of Homerâ€™s Odyssey, book ï™…ï™‡. In
[L437] this scene, Hermes guides the freshly slaughtered suitors of Penelope to the
[L438] netherworld. Mindless, the suitors squeak like bats on their way to the
[L439] halls of Hades.
[L440] Yet there was a more basic similarity between Hermes and Thoth.
[L441] Hermes is more than a herald or messenger. He is the Logos â€“ the Reason,
[L442] Speech, or Word of God.ï™„ï™† The Word devises speech and brings his own
[L443] interpretation, which the Greeks called hermeneia.
[L444] ï™„ï™‡ Thoth is also the god
[L445] who presides over speech and interpretation. He is called â€œthe heart of Re,
[L446] the tongue of Atum, the throat of the God whose name is hidden.â€ As
[L447] ï™„ï™ƒ The â€œwinged son of kindly Maiaâ€ derives from Horace, Odes, ï™„.ï™†. ï™„ï™„ Other similarities between Thoth and Hermes are catalogued by Maria-Theresia Derchain-Urtel,

[L472] Greeks typically conceived of Hermes Thrice Great in a Euhemeristic
[L473] fashion. That is to say, they often considered him to have been an ancient
[L474] man â€“ a real scribe of a real Pharaoh, often the first divine Pharaoh called
[L475] Ammon (the Egyptian god Amun). This scribe, named Thoth or Theuth,

[L477] been using writing to preserve the vast array of accumulated knowledge.
[L478] Thoth was later deified to become a recognized Egyptian god or daimon (a
[L479] kind of mediating deity).ï™„ï™Š Writing was the best-known benefit that
[L480] Thoth offered to human beings, but it was not the only one.

[L1] In his view, awareness is
[L2] removed from our physical bodies only by entering another body, invisibly

[L5] tÃªs psuchÃªs), and it is through this vehicle that the soul animates the physical

[L8] ochÃªma that the soul receives the gods and becomes divine. If the Platonic

[L14] The doctrine of the subtle body in Iamblichus has been carefully examï¿¾ined by John Finamore and, while I will focus specifically on its physical
[L15] sensations in theurgy, his summary will help us proceed. Finamore says:
[L16] For Neoplatonists, the vehicle fulfills three functions: it houses the rational
[L17] soul in its descent from the noetic realm to the realm of generation; it acts as
[L18] the organ of sense-perception and imagination; and, through theurgic rites,
[L19] it can be purified and lifted above, a vehicle for the rational soulâ€™s return
[L20] through the cosmos to the gods.10
[L21] Finamore outlines the Platonic sources of this doctrine: the Timaeus (41e)
[L22] where the Demiurge places souls in starry vehicles (ochÃªmata) and the Phaeï¿¾drus (247b) where the chariots of souls are, again, described as ochÃªmata.
[L23] Perhaps more significantly, as regards physical sensation, is Aristotleâ€™s theï¿¾ory (De Gen. An. 736b)â€”also noted by Finamoreâ€”that each soul has a
[L24] pneumatic body made like the heavenly aether to serve as intermediary
[L25] between the immaterial soul and the physical senses. As Finamore puts it:
[L26] â€œâ€¦ it is a small step for later philosophers to combine Aristotleâ€™s pneuma
[L27] with ether, the element of the stars, and with the â€˜Platonicâ€™ ochÃªma onto
[L28] which the Demiurge placed the soul.â€11 Thus, for Iamblichus, souls have an
[L29] eternal aetheric vehicle that animates the body with breath (pneuma) and
[L30] coordinates sense impressions. The soulâ€™s vehicle is also associated with
[L31] phantasia, and this follows Aristotleâ€™s belief that mental images are necesï¿¾sary for engaging the world. The imagination, like the ochÃªma itself, funcï¿¾tions as a kind of intermediary between material and immaterial realms.
[L32] If it is through the ochÃªma that the soul becomes a god, it necessarily
[L33] plays a critical role in theurgic divination.

[L3] Priscian says that according to Iamblichus imagination translates noetic
[L4] impressions into sensate and divided images. He explains:
[L5] Although imagination receives impressions from all the superior activities
[L6] [noetic wholes], it represents them in images divisibly and in sensible forms
[L7] (Î±á¼°ÏƒÎ¸Î·Ï„Î¹Îºá½°) that refer back to sensible objects (Î±á¼°ÏƒÎ¸Î·Ï„á½°); therefore, while

[L9] it is moved by sensible forms.48
[L10] For Iamblichus, to animate a body is to clothe noetic forms in matter, to
[L11] bring them forth in sensate imagery. The human soul identifies with its
[L12] animal life; it lives entirely in sensation: embodiment is literally â€œbreathed
[L13] in.â€ In this process, the ochÃªma becomes dense and the imagination filled
[L14] with habits of animal life. Because our impulses trap us in the oppositions
[L15] of the sublunary world, theurgists must cleanse the pneumatic body of those
[L16] habits that Damascius calls the â€œstain of generative acts.â€ But how? The
[L17] discursively obvious answer is to reject the life of sensation, and this is
[L18] how we have interpreted theurgic anagÃ´gÃª, making it a literal ascent and
[L19] escape from material life. But for Iamblichus, the answer is that the soulâ€™s
[L20] apotheosis is realized as the descent of a god into flesh and was achieved
[L21] by careful attention to the physical body. Reflecting this theurgic practice

[L27] We must take care of the purity relating to our luminous body (augoeides),

[L29] Such purity extends to our food, our drink, and to the entire regimen of our morï¿¾tal body in which the luminous body resides, breathing life into the inanimate
[L30] body and maintaining its harmony. For the immaterial body is a kind of life,

[L33] The life of the theurgist aimed at nothing short of the transformation of his
[L34] or her body into the body of a god, in eating, drinking, and in prayer. Prayer
[L35] perfects the pneumatic body, making it porous and rarified, which gives us

[L38] It is important to remember that Platonists and Pythagoreans are not
[L39] dualists; there is no opposition between noetic and sensible worlds. Their
[L40] universe is asymmetrical, not symmetrical. Noetic wholes remain present in
[L41] sensible particulars just as primary numbers are present in their multiples:
[L42] for Pythagoreans the immaterial is always present in matter.51 Iamblichusâ€™
[L43] contribution to his tradition was in making this metaphysical law explicit.

[L1] Each of these elements serve as a covering for these bodies. Every
[L2] soul in the body is weighed down and oppressed by these four elements.
[L3] Accordingly, it is natural that the soul likes some elements and dislikes
[L4] others. For this reason, the soul does not enjoy the height of its happiness.
[L5] Rather, because the soul is divine by nature, it struggles amidst these
[L6] elements. The soul has understanding, to be sure, but does not understand
[L7] the things it would if untethered from bodies. Whenever bodies experience
[L8] restlessness and turmoil, whether from disease or fear, then the soul itself is
[L9] heaved by waves â€“ like a person in the open sea â€“ and produces nothing

[L1] Finamore says:
[L2] For Neoplatonists, the vehicle fulfills three functions: it houses the rational
[L3] soul in its descent from the noetic realm to the realm of generation; it acts as
[L4] the organ of sense-perception and imagination; and, through theurgic rites,
[L5] it can be purified and lifted above, a vehicle for the rational soulâ€™s return
[L6] through the cosmos to the gods.10
[L7] Finamore outlines the Platonic sources of this doctrine: the Timaeus (41e)
[L8] where the Demiurge places souls in starry vehicles (ochÃªmata) and the Phaeï¿¾drus (247b) where the chariots of souls are, again, described as ochÃªmata.
[L9] Perhaps more significantly, as regards physical sensation, is Aristotleâ€™s theï¿¾ory (De Gen. An. 736b)â€”also noted by Finamoreâ€”that each soul has a
[L10] pneumatic body made like the heavenly aether to serve as intermediary
[L11] between the immaterial soul and the physical senses. As Finamore puts it:
[L12] â€œâ€¦ it is a small step for later philosophers to combine Aristotleâ€™s pneuma
[L13] with ether, the element of the stars, and with the â€˜Platonicâ€™ ochÃªma onto
[L14] which the Demiurge placed the soul.â€11 Thus, for Iamblichus, souls have an
[L15] eternal aetheric vehicle that animates the body with breath (pneuma) and
[L16] coordinates sense impressions. The soulâ€™s vehicle is also associated with
[L17] phantasia, and this follows Aristotleâ€™s belief that mental images are necesï¿¾sary for engaging the world. The imagination, like the ochÃªma itself, funcï¿¾tions as a kind of intermediary between material and immaterial realms.
[L18] If it is through the ochÃªma that the soul becomes a god, it necessarily
[L19] plays a critical role in theurgic divination. Fortunately, Iamblichus provides
[L20] quite a few clues about how this transformation happens, but it is easy
[L21] to misunderstand what he says. As scholars we are far more the heirs of
[L22] Porphyry than of Iamblichus, and to grasp the theurgic function of the
[L23] ochÃªma we naturally look for discursively coherent descriptions. Porphyry
[L24] did just that. He asked Iamblichus for a diarthrÃ´sis, a precise articulation

[L26] but Iamblichus objects to the question and chastises Porphyry for assuming
[L27] that divination could be understood as if it were a natural phenomenon or
[L28] human technique capable of discursive analysis.12 He says:

[L3] We must take care of the purity relating to our luminous body (augoeides),
[L4] which the Oracles call â€œthe light vehicle of the soulâ€ (psuchÃªs lepton ochÃªma).
[L5] Such purity extends to our food, our drink, and to the entire regimen of our morï¿¾tal body in which the luminous body resides, breathing life into the inanimate
[L6] body and maintaining its harmony. For the immaterial body is a kind of life,
[L7] which also engenders life within matter; it is thanks to this last life that that
[L8] part of myself that is the living mortal being is made complete â€¦49
[L9] The life of the theurgist aimed at nothing short of the transformation of his
[L10] or her body into the body of a god, in eating, drinking, and in prayer. Prayer
[L11] perfects the pneumatic body, making it porous and rarified, which gives us
[L12] the capacity to receive the gods. Our bodies become their organs: the gods
[L13] reveal themselves (ekphainesthai) through us.50
[L14] It is important to remember that Platonists and Pythagoreans are not
[L15] dualists; there is no opposition between noetic and sensible worlds. Their
[L16] universe is asymmetrical, not symmetrical. Noetic wholes remain present in
[L17] sensible particulars just as primary numbers are present in their multiples:
[L18] for Pythagoreans the immaterial is always present in matter.51 Iamblichusâ€™
[L19] contribution to his tradition was in making this metaphysical law explicit.
[L20] Theurgy is its ritual praxis: the ritual reception and expression of the superï¿¾natural in nature. The noetic whole is recovered, not by abandoning parï¿¾ticularsâ€”including our own bodiesâ€”but by realizing, in theurgy, that we
[L21] are creating them continually. According to Iamblichus this noetic/theurgic
[L22] demiurgy is the essential function of the soul: â€œto mediate divisible and indiï¿¾visible, corporeal and incorporeal beings, to receive universal ratios (logoi)
[L23] and serve the work of creation.â€52 To abandon sublunary life in order to
[L24] â€œascendâ€ to the gods is a failure to make this demiurgicâ€”and theurgicâ€”
[L25] turn in which sensate particulars are recognized as creations of oneâ€™s own
[L26] demiurgic will. The soulâ€™s encounter with the gods, then, does not abandon
[L27] aesthetic experience, for there is no other way to recover our divinity. If we
[L28] miss this, we miss the heart of Iamblichean theurgy.

[L2] The Destiny of Souls after Death
[L3] What subsists and does not pass away occupies a realm. For souls released
[L4] from bodies do not pour forth in a jumbled heap into the air, my
[L5] wondrously great child of a great father Osiris, dispersed amidst all the
[L6] remaining boundless breath.ï™… In this case, these souls would no longer be
[L7] able to go back into bodies with their identities preserved. Nor would they
[L8] return any more to that realm from where they came at first. In the same
[L9] way, water received from uplifted jars cannot flow back to the same place
[L10] from which it was taken.ï™† Nor does water occupy its own place after being
[L11] taken and poured out. Rather, it is mixed with the confused mass of liquid.
[L12] ï™‡. The release of souls is not like this, high-minded Horus. Now as an

[L14] Truth, I will relate to you the actual realities in each single detail.ï™‡
[L15] This I will say first, that water is a body lacking reason, compressed from
[L16] many compounds into a confused mass of liquid. The soul, by contrast, is

[L11] <Divine> Necessity, by which things Planned by God Inevitably

[L21] After an introductory formula, Tat asks Hermes to distinguish the forces
[L22] of Providence, Necessity, and Fate. Hermes answers the question in a
[L23] roundabout way by distinguishing three kinds of bodiless entities separï¿¾ately governed by these three forces. Providence governs intellectual reality
[L24] (elsewhere called nous); Necessity governs non-rational forces (like drive
[L25] and desire), and Fate governs the incidental properties of bodies (qualities
[L26] like shape, color, place, and time).
[L27] Nous is free from the forces of Necessity and Fate when in direct
[L28] relation to God. When separated from God, nous falls into a body
[L29] according to the will of Providence. Nous is then joined to the nonï¿¾rational parts of the soul (namely, drive and desire) which are subject
[L30] to Necessity. Insofar as nous is joined with these lower parts of the
[L31] soul, it falls under the power of Necessity. When the human nous
[L32] turns toward God, however, it can both save the lower soul and
[L33] transcend the power of Necessity.

[L1] It is immediately preceded by selections from Iamblichusâ€™s
[L2] treatise On the Soul. It is directly followed by what appears below as SH ï™…ï™‰.
[L3] SH ï™…ï™ˆ seems to assume, and perhaps originally followed, SH ï™…ï™† (the
[L4] KorÄ“ Kosmou). The heart of the KorÄ“ Kosmou tells the story of the soulsâ€™
[L5] creation and embodiment. Accordingly, Horus first thanks Isis for telling
[L6] him about the soulsâ€™ embodiment (SH ï™…ï™ˆ.ï™„). He wants to know what
[L7] happens immediately after souls depart from their bodies.
[L8] Strongly rejected here is the Epicurean idea that souls are dispersed like
[L9] smoke. Dispersal is impossible, given that the soul is simple (made up of a
[L10] single substance), immortal, and divine. In addition, although the soul was
[L11] made from divine breath, it exists as something qualitatively different than
[L12] air. Thus it retains its integrity as it soars through the air, just like water
[L13] runs over oil.
[L14] The more precise question, then, is what realm or realms souls occupy
[L15] after death. As it turns out, the soul occupies one of the levels of air that
[L16] exist between the moon and earth. It naturally ascends to its own level, just
[L17] as creatures of sky, water, and land seek their own natural element. There
[L18] are sixty distinct levels of air, just as there are sixty grades of souls in the
[L19] KorÄ“ Kosmou (ï™…ï™†.ï™„ï™‰). The air strata have four main divisions in which
[L20] there are four, eight, sixteen, and thirty-two subdivisions as one ascends
[L21] higher (Â§Â§ï™„ï™„â€“ï™„ï™†). The height to which the soul ascends depends on the
[L22] soulâ€™s nobility. This nobility hinges, at least in part, on the soulâ€™s choices
[L23] during bodily life.

[L1] 19 This, of course, is the crux of the issue. I believe that exoterically we think of the ochÃªma
[L2] as going up, but this metaphorical ascent releases the divine presence in us that becomes
[L3] more real, more present, and more embodied. In becoming â€œlike the divineâ€ mortals contact
[L4] the immortal and by participation are made divine, filled with piety and wonder. And in
[L5] this transformative experience of the mortal â€œgoing upâ€ to immortality, at a distinctively
[L6] theurgic and pivotal moment, the perspective shifts: it is no longer the mortal reaching up to
[L7] immortality but the other way around. The divine takes on a mortal body and this descent
[L8] is entirely dependent on providing a receptacle, a porous ochÃªma to receive the divine
[L9] light. The ascent and the descent of the soul, while discursively distinct, are esoterically
[L10] simultaneous and co-present. I wish to thank Peter Durigon for this insight.<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 108 / 212>the role of aesthesis in theurgy 97
[L11] The Asclepian sanctuaries, the oracles at Delphi, Claros, and Didymos,
[L12] and the example of ecstatics immune to painfrom knives andfire all demonï¿¾strate the effects of theurgic possession. But Iamblichus seems almost disï¿¾missive of these extraordinary examples andâ€œfar-famed oracles.â€20He seems
[L13] more interested in the divination that was the everyday experience of those
[L14] who live in communion with the gods.
[L15] The daily practice of Iamblichus was prayer. He says plainly that no
[L16] theurgic ritual can take place without it21 and that in prayer the ochÃªma is
[L17] purified. He explains:
[L18] The extended practice of prayer nurtures our intellect, greatly enlarges our
[L19] soulâ€™s receptacle of the gods, reveals to us the life of gods, accustoms our
[L20] eyes to the brightness of divine light, and gradually perfects our capacity for
[L21] intimate union with the gods â€¦ It gently elevates our habits of thought and
[L22] gives us those of the gods â€¦ It increases divine love and inflames the divine
[L23] presence of the soul; it cleanses (á¼€Ï€Î¿ÎºÎ±Î¸Î±á½·ÏÎµÎ¹) all contrary tendencies of the
[L24] soul and removes from its aethereal and luminous vehicle (Î±á¼°Î¸ÎµÏá½½Î´Î¿Ï…Ï‚ ÎºÎ±á½¶
[L25] Î±á½Î³Î¿ÎµÎ¹Î´Î¿á¿¦Ï‚ Ï€Î½Îµá½»ÂµÎ±Ï„Î¿Ï‚) everything inclined to generation â€¦ It makes those
[L26] who pray, if we may express it, the companions of the gods (á½ÂµÎ¹Î»Î·Ï„á½°Ï‚ Ï„á¿¶Î½
[L27] Î¸Îµá¿¶Î½).22
[L28] To bear the light of the gods we must become godlike. In theurgic prayer the
[L29] soul is released from the oppositions of embodied life: our divided dianoia
[L30] is replaced by unified noÃªsis and the contrary tendencies of the pneumatic
[L31] body are also unified, which is to say our pneumatic body is made spherical,
[L32] like the bodies of the heavenly gods. As Iamblichus puts it:
[L33] The aethereal body [of heavenly gods] is exempt from all contrariety and
[L34] is free from every change â€¦ it is utterly liberated from any centripetal or
[L35] centrifugal tendency because it has neither tendency or because it is moved
[L36] in a circle.

[L190] Preface
[L191] Despite widespread interest in the Hermetica across the globe, currently
[L192] there does not exist a reliable and up-to-date English translation of the
[L193] various Hermetic fragments and testimonies. Indeed, some of these fragï¿¾ments and testimonies remain generally unknown.
[L194] At the turn of the twentieth century, G. R. S. Mead made a translation
[L195] of select fragments into Victorian English from now-outdated editions.
[L196] The translation of the fragments by Walter Scott (ï™„ï™Œï™…ï™‡) was based on his
[L197] own re-written Greek text â€“ a jungle of emendations and transpositions.
[L198] When it came to testimonies, moreover, Scott did not actually translate the
[L199] Greek or Latin texts. He only rendered into English (via Latin translations)
[L200] texts originally written in Arabic.
[L201] The present translation serves a new generation of scholarly and lay
[L202] readers of the Hermetica. It remains faithful to â€“ though critically engaged
[L203] with â€“ the various manuscript traditions. Copious notes provide historical
[L204] background, parallels, and references for further reading. Included also are
[L205] many testimonies that Scott did not print. It is hoped that this volume will
[L206] spark interest in the full reception history of the Hermetica, which must
[L207] include Late Antiquity and the medieval period.
[L208] Here I gratefully acknowledge persons who read parts of the manuscript
[L209] and offered helpful suggestions: Brian P. Copenhaver, David Runia, and
[L210] Christian H. Bull. Christian Wildberg and Kevin Van Bladel also kindly
[L211] answered my inquiries and provided guidance based on their expertise. My
[L212] thanks also to Oxford University Press for the use of Van Bladelâ€™s translaï¿¾tions of Arabic source materials.

[L299] General Introduction
[L300] There are five groups of philosophical Hermetic writings that do not
[L301] appear in Brian P. Copenhaverâ€™s ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™… English translation entitled Hermeï¿¾tica: The Greek â€œCorpus Hermeticumâ€ and the Latin â€œAsclepiusâ€.
[L302] ï™„ The first is
[L303] the large group of Hermetic excerpts from Stobaeus, an early fifth-century
[L304] ï£ï¥ anthologist. The second is the Coptic Hermetica (discovered in ï™„ï™Œï™‡ï™ˆ)
[L305] featuring two excerpts of previously known writings in addition to a
[L306] formerly unknown Hermetic tractate (the Discourse on the Eighth and
[L307] Ninth). The third group is the collection of Hermetic Definitions, a set
[L308] of maxims extant in Greek fragments but preserved only fully in Armeï¿¾nian. The fourth is the previously known set of diverse fragments quoted
[L309] by various (largely Christian) authors in Late Antiquity. The fifth comï¿¾prises recently discovered Hermetic fragments currently preserved in
[L310] Vienna and Oxford.
[L311] The Coptic Hermetica are widely available in English translations of the
[L312] Nag Hammadi library.ï™… The Definitions are now conveniently accessible in
[L313] English thanks to the work of Jean-Pierre MahÃ©.ï™† What remains to be
[L314] translated are the fragments from Stobaeus, the fragments and testimonies
[L315] from various authors, and the fragments from the newly discovered papyri.

[L317] Kingsley, â€œAn Introduction to the Hermetica: Approaching Ancient Esoteric Tradition,â€ in Roelof

[L334] It is high time to present a new translation and annotation of these (chiefly
[L335] philosophical) Hermetica to the English-speaking world.
[L336] Hermes-Thoth
[L337] Nothing binds together the multifarious Hermetic fragments beyond their
[L338] ascription to Hermes Thrice Great. Hermes Thrice Great is a fictional
[L339] character. Yet for many in the ancient world that fiction was history. If we
[L340] call Hermes Thrice Great a â€œmyth,â€ we thereby recognize that he is greater
[L341] and more significant than any one historical figure. How do we introduce a
[L342] figure that appears in so many different ages in so many different guises? If
[L343] there was an â€œoriginalâ€ Hermes Thrice Great, we are obliged to pick up the
[L344] thread at significant points of reception.
[L345] Iamblichus (about ï™…ï™‡ï™ˆâ€“ï™†ï™…ï™ˆ ï£ï¥) commences his book (later called On
[L346] the Mysteries) with the following flourish:
[L347] Hermes, the deity who presides over rational discourses, has long and
[L348] rightly been considered common to all who practice the sacred arts. He
[L349] who presides over true science concerning gods is one and the same
[L350] throughout the universe. It is to him that our ancestors dedicated the
[L351] discoveries of their wisdom, attributing all their own writings to Hermes.ï™‡
[L352] Important here is the frank acknowledgement that many authors wrote
[L353] under the name of Hermes. The practice of pseudepigraphy was logical for
[L354] devotees of Hermes.ï™ˆ True wisdom and learning merited ascription to the
[L355] lord of all learning. This is why many Egyptian scholars attributed their
[L356] writings to Hermes. Iamblichus, himself writing under a false name
[L357] (â€œAbammon,â€ an Egyptian priest), calls these writers his â€œancestors.â€ These
[L358] â€œancestorsâ€ were probably Hellenized Egyptian scribes and priests who
[L359] lived not very long before Iamblichus himself.
[L360] Later, Iamblichus gives a taste of how many persons were writing under
[L361] the name of Hermes. In On the Mysteries ï™‹.ï™„, he passes on the report of a
[L362] certain Seleucus, who attributed to Hermes a total of ï™…ï™ƒ,ï™ƒï™ƒï™ƒ books.
[L363] A better-known source, the Egyptian priest and historian Manetho, nearly
[L364] ï™‡ Iamblichus, On the Mysteries, ï™„.ï™„. ï™ˆ On Hermetic pseudepigraphy, see Anthony Grafton, Forgers and Critics: Creativity and Duplicity in
[L365] Western Scholarship (Princeton: Princeton University Press, ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™ƒ), ï™‹ï™ˆâ€“ï™Œï™‹; and more generally, Bart
[L366] D. Ehrman, Forgery and Counterforgery: The Use of Literary Deceit in Early Christian Polemics
[L367] (Oxford: Oxford University Press, ï™…ï™ƒï™„ï™†), ï™„ï™„â€“ï™„ï™‡ï™ˆ; ï™ˆï™†ï™‡â€“ï™‡ï™‹.

[L375] doubled this number, crediting Hermes with ï™†ï™‰,ï™ˆï™…ï™ˆ volumes (or
[L376] ï™„ï™ƒï™ƒ volumes for each single day in a ï™‡-year period).ï™‰ So many books
[L377] would easily fill the shelves of a large temple library (the Egyptian â€œHouse
[L378] of Lifeâ€). Perhaps this is the point â€“ all the wisdom of the Egyptian sacred
[L379] priesthood ultimately reverts to Hermes.
[L380] According to Iamblichus, Hermes wrote hundreds of tomes on specialï¿¾ized topics like â€œthe gods in the fiery zone,â€ â€œthe gods in the aether,â€ and
[L381] â€œthe gods in the heavens.â€
[L382] ï™Š Hermes was the ultimate theologian, yet the
[L383] scope of his expertise was in fact more vast. There are existing treatises on
[L384] astrology, the virtues of stones, the principles of creation, the origin and
[L385] nature of the soul, alchemical practices, Fate, the effects of climate on
[L386] intelligence, healing, and even why children resemble their parents â€“ all
[L387] ascribed to Hermes.ï™‹
[L388] The many genres of Hermetic learning are well illustrated by a passage
[L389] in Clement of Alexandria. This Christian writer around ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒ ï£ï¥ describes a
[L390] procession of Egyptian officials in which forty-two fundamental writings of
[L391] Hermes were displayed (the number of Egyptâ€™s districts or â€œnomesâ€). The
[L392] highest-ranking priest, whom the Greeks called â€œProphet,â€ carried the ten
[L393] â€œhieraticâ€ books on laws, the gods, and the training of priests. The Stoleï¿¾keeper presented ten books on education and sacrifice. The Sacred Scribe
[L394] held up a decade of books on hieroglyphs, geography, and the temples.
[L395] Then came the Astrologer, who showed four books on astronomical
[L396] matters (fixed stars, planets, conjunctions, and the risings of astral bodies).
[L397] Finally, the Singer held in his hands a songbook and an instruction manual
[L398] for kings. As a supplement, six books on medical matters (anatomy,
[L399] medicines, medical instruments, and gynecology) were displayed.ï™Œ All this
[L400] vast store of knowledge was ascribed to Hermes.
[L401] Who was this Hermes? We must first of all distinguish a Greek deity
[L402] from a significantly different Egyptian one. The Greek Hermes was the
[L403] â€œwinged son of kindly Maia,â€ racing on the winds as Zeusâ€™s crafty herald,

[L419] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 18 / 272>
[L420] helmeted with the cap of invisibility, wielding the twisted caduceus with
[L421] the power to put even Argus with his hundred eyes to sleep.ï™„ï™ƒ The
[L422] Egyptian Hermes, on the other hand, was Thoth, depicted as the ibisï¿¾headed scribe of the gods, secretary of Re, giver of oracles, master of magic,
[L423] lord of the moon often appearing in the form of a dog-faced baboon.
[L424] When the Greeks dominated Egypt, they identified the Egyptian god
[L425] Thoth with Hermes. But why? What did they see in these two gods that
[L426] was similar?
[L427] There are several overlaps, yet we will focus on two.ï™„ï™„ First of all,
[L428] Hermes as psychopomp, or escort of the dead, resembled Thoth as seen in
[L429] the various versions of the Egyptian Book of the Dead (or the Book of Going
[L430] Forth by Day), chapter ï™„ï™…ï™ˆ. In the Hall of Two Truths, Thoth hears the
[L431] confession of the deceased person. After the personâ€™s heart is weighed
[L432] against the feather of Maat (â€œJustice,â€ or â€œTruthâ€), Thoth carefully
[L433] inscribes the result with his tablet and stylus. If the heart is pure, Thoth
[L434] leads the candidate into the presence of Osiris, the ultimate judge of the
[L435] dead, and finally into the Field of Reeds.ï™„ï™… Perhaps the most famous scene
[L436] of Hermes leading souls is the opening of Homerâ€™s Odyssey, book ï™…ï™‡. In
[L437] this scene, Hermes guides the freshly slaughtered suitors of Penelope to the
[L438] netherworld. Mindless, the suitors squeak like bats on their way to the
[L439] halls of Hades.
[L440] Yet there was a more basic similarity between Hermes and Thoth.

[L467] In the Hellenistic period (roughly ï™†ï™…ï™†â€“ï™†ï™„ ï¢ï£ï¥), Greeks living in Egypt
[L468] and Hellenized Egyptians crafted an amalgam of Thoth and Hermes who

[L470] (Trismegistos, Termaximus). This Hermes is superlatively great in a superlaï¿¾tive (threefold) way. Following English convention, however, here we call
[L471] â€œThrice Greatest Hermesâ€ simply â€œHermes Thrice Great.â€
[L472] Greeks typically conceived of Hermes Thrice Great in a Euhemeristic
[L473] fashion. That is to say, they often considered him to have been an ancient
[L474] man â€“ a real scribe of a real Pharaoh, often the first divine Pharaoh called
[L475] Ammon (the Egyptian god Amun). This scribe, named Thoth or Theuth,
[L476] invented the alphabet and the art of writing.ï™„ï™‰ Ever since, humans have
[L477] been using writing to preserve the vast array of accumulated knowledge.
[L478] Thoth was later deified to become a recognized Egyptian god or daimon (a
[L479] kind of mediating deity).ï™„ï™Š Writing was the best-known benefit that
[L480] Thoth offered to human beings, but it was not the only one.
[L481] Greeks attributed to Thoth the invention of a host of other arts. Plato
[L482] (ï™‡ï™…ï™Šâ€“ï™†ï™‡ï™Š ï¢ï£ï¥) made Thoth the discoverer of mathematics and astronï¿¾omy.
[L483] ï™„ï™‹ Hecataeus of Abdera (fourth century ï¢ï£ï¥) ascribed to him the
[L484] invention of a common language, religious ritual, music, wrestling,
[L485] dancing, and the culture of the olive.ï™„ï™Œ The Jewish writer Artapanus (third

[L190] Preface
[L191] Despite widespread interest in the Hermetica across the globe, currently
[L192] there does not exist a reliable and up-to-date English translation of the
[L193] various Hermetic fragments and testimonies. Indeed, some of these fragï¿¾ments and testimonies remain generally unknown.
[L194] At the turn of the twentieth century, G. R. S. Mead made a translation
[L195] of select fragments into Victorian English from now-outdated editions.
[L196] The translation of the fragments by Walter Scott (ï™„ï™Œï™…ï™‡) was based on his
[L197] own re-written Greek text â€“ a jungle of emendations and transpositions.
[L198] When it came to testimonies, moreover, Scott did not actually translate the
[L199] Greek or Latin texts. He only rendered into English (via Latin translations)
[L200] texts originally written in Arabic.
[L201] The present translation serves a new generation of scholarly and lay
[L202] readers of the Hermetica. It remains faithful to â€“ though critically engaged
[L203] with â€“ the various manuscript traditions. Copious notes provide historical
[L204] background, parallels, and references for further reading. Included also are
[L205] many testimonies that Scott did not print. It is hoped that this volume will
[L206] spark interest in the full reception history of the Hermetica, which must
[L207] include Late Antiquity and the medieval period.
[L208] Here I gratefully acknowledge persons who read parts of the manuscript
[L209] and offered helpful suggestions: Brian P. Copenhaver, David Runia, and
[L210] Christian H. Bull. Christian Wildberg and Kevin Van Bladel also kindly
[L211] answered my inquiries and provided guidance based on their expertise. My
[L212] thanks also to Oxford University Press for the use of Van Bladelâ€™s translaï¿¾tions of Arabic source materials.

[L299] General Introduction
[L300] There are five groups of philosophical Hermetic writings that do not
[L301] appear in Brian P. Copenhaverâ€™s ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™… English translation entitled Hermeï¿¾tica: The Greek â€œCorpus Hermeticumâ€ and the Latin â€œAsclepiusâ€.
[L302] ï™„ The first is
[L303] the large group of Hermetic excerpts from Stobaeus, an early fifth-century
[L304] ï£ï¥ anthologist. The second is the Coptic Hermetica (discovered in ï™„ï™Œï™‡ï™ˆ)
[L305] featuring two excerpts of previously known writings in addition to a
[L306] formerly unknown Hermetic tractate (the Discourse on the Eighth and
[L307] Ninth). The third group is the collection of Hermetic Definitions, a set
[L308] of maxims extant in Greek fragments but preserved only fully in Armeï¿¾nian. The fourth is the previously known set of diverse fragments quoted
[L309] by various (largely Christian) authors in Late Antiquity. The fifth comï¿¾prises recently discovered Hermetic fragments currently preserved in
[L310] Vienna and Oxford.
[L311] The Coptic Hermetica are widely available in English translations of the
[L312] Nag Hammadi library.ï™… The Definitions are now conveniently accessible in
[L313] English thanks to the work of Jean-Pierre MahÃ©.ï™† What remains to be
[L314] translated are the fragments from Stobaeus, the fragments and testimonies
[L315] from various authors, and the fragments from the newly discovered papyri.
[L316] ï™„ Copenhaverâ€™s introduction to CH and Ascl. remain relevant (Copenhaver, xxxiiâ€“xlv). See also Peter
[L317] Kingsley, â€œAn Introduction to the Hermetica: Approaching Ancient Esoteric Tradition,â€ in Roelof
[L318] van den Broek and Cis van Heertum, eds., From Poimandres to Jacob BÃ¶hme: Gnosis: Hermetism and
[L319] the Christian Tradition (Amsterdam: In de Pelikaan, ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒï™ƒ), ï™„ï™Šâ€“ï™‡ï™ƒ. ï™… See Marvin Meyer, ed., Nag Hammadi Scriptures: The International Edition (New York: HarperOne,
[L320] ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒï™‹), ï™‡ï™ƒï™Œâ€“ï™†ï™‰. Introductions to the Coptic Hermetica can be found in ibid., ï™‡ï™ƒï™Œâ€“ï™„ï™…; ï™‡ï™„ï™Œâ€“ï™…ï™„;
[L321] ï™‡ï™…ï™ˆâ€“ï™…ï™Œ; MahÃ©, HHE, ï™„.ï™†ï™„â€“ï™ˆï™„, ï™„ï™†ï™Šâ€“ï™ˆï™ˆ; ï™….ï™‡ï™Šâ€“ï™„ï™‡ï™‡; Hans-Martin Schenke, Hans-Gebhard Bethge,

[L334] It is high time to present a new translation and annotation of these (chiefly
[L335] philosophical) Hermetica to the English-speaking world.
[L336] Hermes-Thoth
[L337] Nothing binds together the multifarious Hermetic fragments beyond their
[L338] ascription to Hermes Thrice Great. Hermes Thrice Great is a fictional
[L339] character. Yet for many in the ancient world that fiction was history. If we
[L340] call Hermes Thrice Great a â€œmyth,â€ we thereby recognize that he is greater
[L341] and more significant than any one historical figure. How do we introduce a
[L342] figure that appears in so many different ages in so many different guises? If
[L343] there was an â€œoriginalâ€ Hermes Thrice Great, we are obliged to pick up the
[L344] thread at significant points of reception.
[L345] Iamblichus (about ï™…ï™‡ï™ˆâ€“ï™†ï™…ï™ˆ ï£ï¥) commences his book (later called On
[L346] the Mysteries) with the following flourish:
[L347] Hermes, the deity who presides over rational discourses, has long and
[L348] rightly been considered common to all who practice the sacred arts. He
[L349] who presides over true science concerning gods is one and the same
[L350] throughout the universe. It is to him that our ancestors dedicated the
[L351] discoveries of their wisdom, attributing all their own writings to Hermes.ï™‡
[L352] Important here is the frank acknowledgement that many authors wrote
[L353] under the name of Hermes. The practice of pseudepigraphy was logical for
[L354] devotees of Hermes.ï™ˆ True wisdom and learning merited ascription to the
[L355] lord of all learning. This is why many Egyptian scholars attributed their
[L356] writings to Hermes. Iamblichus, himself writing under a false name
[L357] (â€œAbammon,â€ an Egyptian priest), calls these writers his â€œancestors.â€ These
[L358] â€œancestorsâ€ were probably Hellenized Egyptian scribes and priests who
[L359] lived not very long before Iamblichus himself.
[L360] Later, Iamblichus gives a taste of how many persons were writing under
[L361] the name of Hermes. In On the Mysteries ï™‹.ï™„, he passes on the report of a
[L362] certain Seleucus, who attributed to Hermes a total of ï™…ï™ƒ,ï™ƒï™ƒï™ƒ books.
[L363] A better-known source, the Egyptian priest and historian Manetho, nearly
[L364] ï™‡ Iamblichus, On the Mysteries, ï™„.ï™„. ï™ˆ On Hermetic pseudepigraphy, see Anthony Grafton, Forgers and Critics: Creativity and Duplicity in
[L365] Western Scholarship (Princeton: Princeton University Press, ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™ƒ), ï™‹ï™ˆâ€“ï™Œï™‹; and more generally, Bart
[L366] D. Ehrman, Forgery and Counterforgery: The Use of Literary Deceit in Early Christian Polemics
[L367] (Oxford: Oxford University Press, ï™…ï™ƒï™„ï™†), ï™„ï™„â€“ï™„ï™‡ï™ˆ; ï™ˆï™†ï™‡â€“ï™‡ï™‹.

[L374] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 17 / 272>
[L375] doubled this number, crediting Hermes with ï™†ï™‰,ï™ˆï™…ï™ˆ volumes (or
[L376] ï™„ï™ƒï™ƒ volumes for each single day in a ï™‡-year period).ï™‰ So many books
[L377] would easily fill the shelves of a large temple library (the Egyptian â€œHouse
[L378] of Lifeâ€). Perhaps this is the point â€“ all the wisdom of the Egyptian sacred
[L379] priesthood ultimately reverts to Hermes.
[L380] According to Iamblichus, Hermes wrote hundreds of tomes on specialï¿¾ized topics like â€œthe gods in the fiery zone,â€ â€œthe gods in the aether,â€ and
[L381] â€œthe gods in the heavens.â€
[L382] ï™Š Hermes was the ultimate theologian, yet the
[L383] scope of his expertise was in fact more vast. There are existing treatises on
[L384] astrology, the virtues of stones, the principles of creation, the origin and
[L385] nature of the soul, alchemical practices, Fate, the effects of climate on
[L386] intelligence, healing, and even why children resemble their parents â€“ all
[L387] ascribed to Hermes.ï™‹
[L388] The many genres of Hermetic learning are well illustrated by a passage
[L389] in Clement of Alexandria. This Christian writer around ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒ ï£ï¥ describes a
[L390] procession of Egyptian officials in which forty-two fundamental writings of
[L391] Hermes were displayed (the number of Egyptâ€™s districts or â€œnomesâ€). The
[L392] highest-ranking priest, whom the Greeks called â€œProphet,â€ carried the ten
[L393] â€œhieraticâ€ books on laws, the gods, and the training of priests. The Stoleï¿¾keeper presented ten books on education and sacrifice. The Sacred Scribe
[L394] held up a decade of books on hieroglyphs, geography, and the temples.
[L395] Then came the Astrologer, who showed four books on astronomical
[L396] matters (fixed stars, planets, conjunctions, and the risings of astral bodies).
[L397] Finally, the Singer held in his hands a songbook and an instruction manual
[L398] for kings. As a supplement, six books on medical matters (anatomy,
[L399] medicines, medical instruments, and gynecology) were displayed.ï™Œ All this
[L400] vast store of knowledge was ascribed to Hermes.
[L401] Who was this Hermes? We must first of all distinguish a Greek deity
[L402] from a significantly different Egyptian one. The Greek Hermes was the
[L403] â€œwinged son of kindly Maia,â€ racing on the winds as Zeusâ€™s crafty herald,

[L420] helmeted with the cap of invisibility, wielding the twisted caduceus with

[L422] Egyptian Hermes, on the other hand, was Thoth, depicted as the ibisï¿¾headed scribe of the gods, secretary of Re, giver of oracles, master of magic,
[L423] lord of the moon often appearing in the form of a dog-faced baboon.
[L424] When the Greeks dominated Egypt, they identified the Egyptian god
[L425] Thoth with Hermes. But why? What did they see in these two gods that
[L426] was similar?
[L427] There are several overlaps, yet we will focus on two.ï™„ï™„ First of all,
[L428] Hermes as psychopomp, or escort of the dead, resembled Thoth as seen in
[L429] the various versions of the Egyptian Book of the Dead (or the Book of Going
[L430] Forth by Day), chapter ï™„ï™…ï™ˆ. In the Hall of Two Truths, Thoth hears the
[L431] confession of the deceased person. After the personâ€™s heart is weighed
[L432] against the feather of Maat (â€œJustice,â€ or â€œTruthâ€), Thoth carefully
[L433] inscribes the result with his tablet and stylus. If the heart is pure, Thoth
[L434] leads the candidate into the presence of Osiris, the ultimate judge of the
[L435] dead, and finally into the Field of Reeds.ï™„ï™… Perhaps the most famous scene
[L436] of Hermes leading souls is the opening of Homerâ€™s Odyssey, book ï™…ï™‡. In
[L437] this scene, Hermes guides the freshly slaughtered suitors of Penelope to the
[L438] netherworld. Mindless, the suitors squeak like bats on their way to the
[L439] halls of Hades.
[L440] Yet there was a more basic similarity between Hermes and Thoth.
[L441] Hermes is more than a herald or messenger. He is the Logos â€“ the Reason,
[L442] Speech, or Word of God.ï™„ï™† The Word devises speech and brings his own
[L443] interpretation, which the Greeks called hermeneia.
[L444] ï™„ï™‡ Thoth is also the god
[L445] who presides over speech and interpretation. He is called â€œthe heart of Re,
[L446] the tongue of Atum, the throat of the God whose name is hidden.â€ As
[L447] ï™„ï™ƒ The â€œwinged son of kindly Maiaâ€ derives from Horace, Odes, ï™„.ï™†. ï™„ï™„ Other similarities between Thoth and Hermes are catalogued by Maria-Theresia Derchain-Urtel,

[L467] In the Hellenistic period (roughly ï™†ï™…ï™†â€“ï™†ï™„ ï¢ï£ï¥), Greeks living in Egypt

[L471] â€œThrice Greatest Hermesâ€ simply â€œHermes Thrice Great.â€
[L472] Greeks typically conceived of Hermes Thrice Great in a Euhemeristic
[L473] fashion. That is to say, they often considered him to have been an ancient
[L474] man â€“ a real scribe of a real Pharaoh, often the first divine Pharaoh called
[L475] Ammon (the Egyptian god Amun). This scribe, named Thoth or Theuth,
[L476] invented the alphabet and the art of writing.ï™„ï™‰ Ever since, humans have
[L477] been using writing to preserve the vast array of accumulated knowledge.
[L478] Thoth was later deified to become a recognized Egyptian god or daimon (a
[L479] kind of mediating deity).ï™„ï™Š Writing was the best-known benefit that
[L480] Thoth offered to human beings, but it was not the only one.
[L481] Greeks attributed to Thoth the invention of a host of other arts. Plato

[L190] Preface
[L191] Despite widespread interest in the Hermetica across the globe, currently
[L192] there does not exist a reliable and up-to-date English translation of the
[L193] various Hermetic fragments and testimonies. Indeed, some of these fragï¿¾ments and testimonies remain generally unknown.
[L194] At the turn of the twentieth century, G. R. S. Mead made a translation
[L195] of select fragments into Victorian English from now-outdated editions.
[L196] The translation of the fragments by Walter Scott (ï™„ï™Œï™…ï™‡) was based on his
[L197] own re-written Greek text â€“ a jungle of emendations and transpositions.
[L198] When it came to testimonies, moreover, Scott did not actually translate the
[L199] Greek or Latin texts. He only rendered into English (via Latin translations)
[L200] texts originally written in Arabic.
[L201] The present translation serves a new generation of scholarly and lay
[L202] readers of the Hermetica. It remains faithful to â€“ though critically engaged
[L203] with â€“ the various manuscript traditions. Copious notes provide historical
[L204] background, parallels, and references for further reading. Included also are
[L205] many testimonies that Scott did not print. It is hoped that this volume will
[L206] spark interest in the full reception history of the Hermetica, which must
[L207] include Late Antiquity and the medieval period.
[L208] Here I gratefully acknowledge persons who read parts of the manuscript
[L209] and offered helpful suggestions: Brian P. Copenhaver, David Runia, and
[L210] Christian H. Bull. Christian Wildberg and Kevin Van Bladel also kindly
[L211] answered my inquiries and provided guidance based on their expertise. My
[L212] thanks also to Oxford University Press for the use of Van Bladelâ€™s translaï¿¾tions of Arabic source materials.

[L299] General Introduction
[L300] There are five groups of philosophical Hermetic writings that do not
[L301] appear in Brian P. Copenhaverâ€™s ï™„ï™Œï™Œï™… English translation entitled Hermeï¿¾tica: The Greek â€œCorpus Hermeticumâ€ and the Latin â€œAsclepiusâ€.
[L302] ï™„ The first is
[L303] the large group of Hermetic excerpts from Stobaeus, an early fifth-century
[L304] ï£ï¥ anthologist. The second is the Coptic Hermetica (discovered in ï™„ï™Œï™‡ï™ˆ)
[L305] featuring two excerpts of previously known writings in addition to a
[L306] formerly unknown Hermetic tractate (the Discourse on the Eighth and
[L307] Ninth). The third group is the collection of Hermetic Definitions, a set
[L308] of maxims extant in Greek fragments but preserved only fully in Armeï¿¾nian. The fourth is the previously known set of diverse fragments quoted
[L309] by various (largely Christian) authors in Late Antiquity. The fifth comï¿¾prises recently discovered Hermetic fragments currently preserved in
[L310] Vienna and Oxford.
[L311] The Coptic Hermetica are widely available in English translations of the
[L312] Nag Hammadi library.ï™… The Definitions are now conveniently accessible in
[L313] English thanks to the work of Jean-Pierre MahÃ©.ï™† What remains to be
[L314] translated are the fragments from Stobaeus, the fragments and testimonies
[L315] from various authors, and the fragments from the newly discovered papyri.
[L316] ï™„ Copenhaverâ€™s introduction to CH and Ascl. remain relevant (Copenhaver, xxxiiâ€“xlv). See also Peter
[L317] Kingsley, â€œAn Introduction to the Hermetica: Approaching Ancient Esoteric Tradition,â€ in Roelof
[L318] van den Broek and Cis van Heertum, eds., From Poimandres to Jacob BÃ¶hme: Gnosis: Hermetism and
[L319] the Christian Tradition (Amsterdam: In de Pelikaan, ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒï™ƒ), ï™„ï™Šâ€“ï™‡ï™ƒ. ï™… See Marvin Meyer, ed., Nag Hammadi Scriptures: The International Edition (New York: HarperOne,
[L320] ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒï™‹), ï™‡ï™ƒï™Œâ€“ï™†ï™‰. Introductions to the Coptic Hermetica can be found in ibid., ï™‡ï™ƒï™Œâ€“ï™„ï™…; ï™‡ï™„ï™Œâ€“ï™…ï™„;
[L321] ï™‡ï™…ï™ˆâ€“ï™…ï™Œ; MahÃ©, HHE, ï™„.ï™†ï™„â€“ï™ˆï™„, ï™„ï™†ï™Šâ€“ï™ˆï™ˆ; ï™….ï™‡ï™Šâ€“ï™„ï™‡ï™‡; Hans-Martin Schenke, Hans-Gebhard Bethge,

[L334] It is high time to present a new translation and annotation of these (chiefly
[L335] philosophical) Hermetica to the English-speaking world.
[L336] Hermes-Thoth
[L337] Nothing binds together the multifarious Hermetic fragments beyond their
[L338] ascription to Hermes Thrice Great. Hermes Thrice Great is a fictional
[L339] character. Yet for many in the ancient world that fiction was history. If we
[L340] call Hermes Thrice Great a â€œmyth,â€ we thereby recognize that he is greater
[L341] and more significant than any one historical figure. How do we introduce a
[L342] figure that appears in so many different ages in so many different guises? If
[L343] there was an â€œoriginalâ€ Hermes Thrice Great, we are obliged to pick up the
[L344] thread at significant points of reception.
[L345] Iamblichus (about ï™…ï™‡ï™ˆâ€“ï™†ï™…ï™ˆ ï£ï¥) commences his book (later called On
[L346] the Mysteries) with the following flourish:
[L347] Hermes, the deity who presides over rational discourses, has long and
[L348] rightly been considered common to all who practice the sacred arts. He
[L349] who presides over true science concerning gods is one and the same
[L350] throughout the universe. It is to him that our ancestors dedicated the
[L351] discoveries of their wisdom, attributing all their own writings to Hermes.ï™‡
[L352] Important here is the frank acknowledgement that many authors wrote
[L353] under the name of Hermes. The practice of pseudepigraphy was logical for
[L354] devotees of Hermes.ï™ˆ True wisdom and learning merited ascription to the
[L355] lord of all learning. This is why many Egyptian scholars attributed their
[L356] writings to Hermes. Iamblichus, himself writing under a false name
[L357] (â€œAbammon,â€ an Egyptian priest), calls these writers his â€œancestors.â€ These
[L358] â€œancestorsâ€ were probably Hellenized Egyptian scribes and priests who
[L359] lived not very long before Iamblichus himself.
[L360] Later, Iamblichus gives a taste of how many persons were writing under
[L361] the name of Hermes. In On the Mysteries ï™‹.ï™„, he passes on the report of a
[L362] certain Seleucus, who attributed to Hermes a total of ï™…ï™ƒ,ï™ƒï™ƒï™ƒ books.
[L363] A better-known source, the Egyptian priest and historian Manetho, nearly
[L364] ï™‡ Iamblichus, On the Mysteries, ï™„.ï™„. ï™ˆ On Hermetic pseudepigraphy, see Anthony Grafton, Forgers and Critics: Creativity and Duplicity in

[L375] doubled this number, crediting Hermes with ï™†ï™‰,ï™ˆï™…ï™ˆ volumes (or

[L380] According to Iamblichus, Hermes wrote hundreds of tomes on specialï¿¾ized topics like â€œthe gods in the fiery zone,â€ â€œthe gods in the aether,â€ and

[L382] ï™Š Hermes was the ultimate theologian, yet the

[L388] The many genres of Hermetic learning are well illustrated by a passage
[L389] in Clement of Alexandria. This Christian writer around ï™…ï™ƒï™ƒ ï£ï¥ describes a
[L390] procession of Egyptian officials in which forty-two fundamental writings of
[L391] Hermes were displayed (the number of Egyptâ€™s districts or â€œnomesâ€). The

[L393] â€œhieraticâ€ books on laws, the gods, and the training of priests. The Stoleï¿¾keeper presented ten books on education and sacrifice. The Sacred Scribe
[L394] held up a decade of books on hieroglyphs, geography, and the temples.
[L395] Then came the Astrologer, who showed four books on astronomical
[L396] matters (fixed stars, planets, conjunctions, and the risings of astral bodies).
[L397] Finally, the Singer held in his hands a songbook and an instruction manual
[L398] for kings. As a supplement, six books on medical matters (anatomy,
[L399] medicines, medical instruments, and gynecology) were displayed.ï™Œ All this
[L400] vast store of knowledge was ascribed to Hermes.

[L403] â€œwinged son of kindly Maia,â€ racing on the winds as Zeusâ€™s crafty herald,

[L420] helmeted with the cap of invisibility, wielding the twisted caduceus with
