[L422] Egyptian Hermes, on the other hand, was Thoth, depicted as the ibisï¿¾headed scribe of the gods, secretary of Re, giver of oracles, master of magic,
[L423] lord of the moon often appearing in the form of a dog-faced baboon.
[L424] When the Greeks dominated Egypt, they identified the Egyptian god
[L425] Thoth with Hermes. But why? What did they see in these two gods that
[L426] was similar?
[L427] There are several overlaps, yet we will focus on two.ï™„ï™„ First of all,
[L428] Hermes as psychopomp, or escort of the dead, resembled Thoth as seen in
[L429] the various versions of the Egyptian Book of the Dead (or the Book of Going
[L430] Forth by Day), chapter ï™„ï™…ï™ˆ. In the Hall of Two Truths, Thoth hears the
[L431] confession of the deceased person. After the personâ€™s heart is weighed
[L432] against the feather of Maat (â€œJustice,â€ or â€œTruthâ€), Thoth carefully
[L433] inscribes the result with his tablet and stylus. If the heart is pure, Thoth
[L434] leads the candidate into the presence of Osiris, the ultimate judge of the
[L435] dead, and finally into the Field of Reeds.ï™„ï™… Perhaps the most famous scene
[L436] of Hermes leading souls is the opening of Homerâ€™s Odyssey, book ï™…ï™‡. In
[L437] this scene, Hermes guides the freshly slaughtered suitors of Penelope to the
[L438] netherworld. Mindless, the suitors squeak like bats on their way to the
[L439] halls of Hades.
[L440] Yet there was a more basic similarity between Hermes and Thoth.
[L441] Hermes is more than a herald or messenger. He is the Logos â€“ the Reason,
[L442] Speech, or Word of God.ï™„ï™† The Word devises speech and brings his own
[L443] interpretation, which the Greeks called hermeneia.
[L444] ï™„ï™‡ Thoth is also the god
[L445] who presides over speech and interpretation. He is called â€œthe heart of Re,
[L446] the tongue of Atum, the throat of the God whose name is hidden.â€ As
[L447] ï™„ï™ƒ The â€œwinged son of kindly Maiaâ€ derives from Horace, Odes, ï™„.ï™†. ï™„ï™„ Other similarities between Thoth and Hermes are catalogued by Maria-Theresia Derchain-Urtel,

[L472] Greeks typically conceived of Hermes Thrice Great in a Euhemeristic
[L473] fashion. That is to say, they often considered him to have been an ancient
[L474] man â€“ a real scribe of a real Pharaoh, often the first divine Pharaoh called
[L475] Ammon (the Egyptian god Amun). This scribe, named Thoth or Theuth,
[L476] invented the alphabet and the art of writing.ï™„ï™‰ Ever since, humans have
[L477] been using writing to preserve the vast array of accumulated knowledge.
[L478] Thoth was later deified to become a recognized Egyptian god or daimon (a
[L479] kind of mediating deity).ï™„ï™Š Writing was the best-known benefit that
[L480] Thoth offered to human beings, but it was not the only one.

Content Snippet:
[L1] Each of these elements serve as a covering for these bodies. Every
[L2] soul in the body is weighed down and oppressed by these four elements.
[L3] Accordingly, it is natural that the soul likes some elements and dislikes
[L4] others. For this reason, the soul does not enjoy the height of its happiness.
[L5] Rather, because the soul is divine by nature, it struggles amidst these
[L6] elements. The soul has understanding, to be sure, but does not understand
[L7] the things it would if untethered from bodies. Whenever bodies experience

[L1] In his view, awareness is
[L2] removed from our physical bodies only by entering another body, invisibly
[L3] present in the physical and activated in moments of ecstatic release. This
[L4] subtle body was described by Iamblichus as the vehicle of the soul (ochÃªma
[L5] tÃªs psuchÃªs), and it is through this vehicle that the soul animates the physical
[L6] body and through which it receives the presence of the gods. It is through
[L7] the ochÃªma that the soul enters embodied life, and it is through this same
[L8] ochÃªma that the soul receives the gods and becomes divine. If the Platonic
[L9] philosopher was to become an embodied god, as Plotinus encouraged, it
[L10] was by means of the ochÃªma.

[L14] The doctrine of the subtle body in Iamblichus has been carefully examï¿¾ined by John Finamore and, while I will focus specifically on its physical
[L15] sensations in theurgy, his summary will help us proceed. Finamore says:
[L16] For Neoplatonists, the vehicle fulfills three functions: it houses the rational
[L17] soul in its descent from the noetic realm to the realm of generation; it acts as
[L18] the organ of sense-perception and imagination; and, through theurgic rites,
[L19] it can be purified and lifted above, a vehicle for the rational soulâ€™s return
[L20] through the cosmos to the gods.10
[L21] Finamore outlines the Platonic sources of this doctrine: the Timaeus (41e)
[L22] where the Demiurge places souls in starry vehicles (ochÃªmata) and the Phaeï¿¾drus (247b) where the chariots of souls are, again, described as ochÃªmata.
[L23] Perhaps more significantly, as regards physical sensation, is Aristotleâ€™s theï¿¾ory (De Gen. An. 736b)â€”also noted by Finamoreâ€”that each soul has a
[L24] pneumatic body made like the heavenly aether to serve as intermediary
[L25] between the immaterial soul and the physical senses. As Finamore puts it:
[L26] â€œâ€¦ it is a small step for later philosophers to combine Aristotleâ€™s pneuma
[L27] with ether, the element of the stars, and with the â€˜Platonicâ€™ ochÃªma onto
[L28] which the Demiurge placed the soul.â€11 Thus, for Iamblichus, souls have an
[L29] eternal aetheric vehicle that animates the body with breath (pneuma) and
[L30] coordinates sense impressions. The soulâ€™s vehicle is also associated with
[L31] phantasia, and this follows Aristotleâ€™s belief that mental images are necesï¿¾sary for engaging the world. The imagination, like the ochÃªma itself, funcï¿¾tions as a kind of intermediary between material and immaterial realms.
[L32] If it is through the ochÃªma that the soul becomes a god, it necessarily
[L33] plays a critical role in theurgic divination.

[L16] For Neoplatonists, the vehicle fulfills three functions: it houses the rational

[L18] the organ of sense-perception and imagination; and, through theurgic rites,

[L23] Perhaps more significantly, as regards physical sensation, is Aristotleâ€™s theï¿¾ory (De Gen. An. 736b)â€”also noted by Finamoreâ€”that each soul has a
[L24] pneumatic body made like the heavenly aether to serve as intermediary

[L1] Human bodies can travel only so far as their
[L2] nature and the astral powers allow (Â§ï™‡ï™‹â€“ï™‡ï™Œ).
[L3] In preparation for human embodiment, a second creation story is told
[L4] (Â§Â§ï™ˆï™ƒâ€“ï™ˆï™…). According to this account, God calls a (second) council of star
[L5] gods. As they once provided their gifts to humankind, so now they offer
[L6] their products to the world. The mist and dark chaos that is lower earth is
[L7] immediately ordered and made visible to the heavens above. God himself
[L8] directly provides all the products of nature to the world system. The earth
[L9] is ready to receive the host of souls.
[L10] Locked up in mortal bodies, the souls prove utterly rebellious and
[L11] barbarous. Remembering their noble birth, humans impiously assault the
[L12] star gods. On earth, the strong ruthlessly oppress the weak. Temples are
[L13] polluted with the bodies of the butchered. There are no laws and no fear of
[L14] punishment (Â§Â§ï™ˆï™†â€“ï™ˆï™‡).
[L15] Four figures rise to call a halt to human savagery. They are the elements:
[L16] Fire, Air, Water, and Earth. Lamenting their defilement, the elements
[L17] petition the supreme God to send a subordinate god to civilize the world
[L18] (Â§Â§ï™ˆï™ˆâ€“ï™‰ï™„). The high God responds by sending his own emanation in the
[L19] form of Isis and Osiris. When they arrive on earth, they fill it with
[L20] the blessings of law, art, and religious rites. They discover and ensure the
[L21] preservation of Hermesâ€™s ancient wisdom. After prayer, Isis and Osiris
[L22] re-ascend â€“ like Hermes of old â€“ to their heavenly home.
[L23] With their return journey complete, the tractate comes to a close. Isis
[L24] herself guarantees that true knowledge of creation will persist on earth by
[L25] passing it on to her son Horus â€“ and hence to all future readers of
[L26] Hermetic lore.

[L1] The tractate
[L2] preceding SH ï™…ï™‡ speaks of sixty different soul grades (SH ï™…ï™†.ï™„ï™ˆâ€“ï™„ï™‰). The
[L3] tractate following it (SH ï™…ï™ˆ.ï™„ï™†) tells of sixty different soul regions.
[L4] SH ï™…ï™‡ states that royal souls descend from a higher level (Â§ï™†) and that there
[L5] are more glorious regions from which nobler souls descend as well (Â§ï™Š).
[L6] The author seems to assume that nobler souls are born as males. He
[L7] admits that the distinction between male and female only makes sense
[L8] when souls are embodied (Â§ï™‹). Nevertheless, the nobility of a soul seems to
[L9] be expressed by its being born in a male body. In section ï™Œ, the author
[L10] appeals to physiological distinctions among male and female bodies to
[L11] naturalize their putative differences in character.
[L12] Horus then asks how intelligent souls arise. Isis responds by comparing
[L13] the soul to the eye. The eye has the innate power of sight, just as the soul
[L14] has the power of intelligence. Nevertheless, the aerial membranes of the
[L15] soul â€“ just as the membranes of the eye â€“ can cloud the power of intellect
[L16] (Â§ï™„ï™ƒ). The soul requires these aerial membranes, apparently, in order to
[L17] inhabit a body.
[L18] Intelligence is also affected by climate. Egypt, as it turns out, has the
[L19] best climate for producing intelligent souls. Egyptâ€™s temperate climate
[L20] depends on its central location on the earthâ€™s surface. A vivid image
[L21] illustrates this centrality (Â§Â§ï™„ï™„â€“ï™„ï™…). Earth is imagined as a woman lying
[L22] on her back. Her head is Ethiopia, her buttocks and thighs constitute
[L23] Greece, and her calves and feet are spread out as northern Europe. This
[L24] leaves the region of her chest or heart aligned directly over Egypt. The
[L25] Egyptians and Stoics agreed that the heart was the location of human
[L26] intelligence and the command center for the entire body. Thus all the
[L27] bodily virtues in which other ethnicities excel Egyptians excel in too â€“ with
[L28] the addition of a keener intelligence.
[L29] It remains to discuss chronic illnesses that lead to dumbness and
[L30] dementia (considered to be diseases of the soul). Isis explains these illnesses
[L31] by her theory of elements. Creatures are naturally adapted toward some
[L32] elements and repelled by others. So also with the divine soul. It prefers and
[L33] rejects some elements, but is at home in none. Thus it must struggle
[L34] continually against bodily ailments.

[L1] This principle of acting that depends on providence
[L2] but not on fate would transcend the latter. In the extract from Iamblichusâ€™
[L3] epistle, as line 23 reveals, this principle is said to reside in the soul: in its
[L4] inferior part, which is subject to fate, as well as in its superior part, which is
[L5] independent of fate.
[L6] The overall structure of the argument presented in eclogue 45a would
[L7] now appear to be largely consistent.
[L8] At lines 9â€“11 we find a principle of human acting describing the condition
[L9] of the embodied soul. This principle by necessity obeys the laws of proviï¿¾dence and fate.
[L10] At lines 11â€“13 this principle is said to imply a principle of individual
[L11] actions that escapes the laws of fate. It is further specified (lines 13â€“16) that
[L12] this principle of individual actions precedes and is more noble than the
[L13] order of fate.
[L14] Then (at lines 16â€“21) the theme of lines 9â€“11 is taken up again and it is
[L15] stated that by the process of generation souls are allotted certain regions of
[L16] the universe, through an act presupposing the totality of the universe.
[L17] It may thus be argued that the first section of the text and the last (lines 9â€“
[L18] 11 and 16â€“21) refer to the condition of souls within the realm of generation
[L19] and describe the ontological condition of embodied souls, whereas the
[L20] remaining sections (lines 11â€“16) point to a specific possibility within this
[L21] condition: the persistence in the soul of the â€˜inputâ€™ of a principle superior
[L22] to the natural order.
[L23] In the light of the above considerations, it is clear that the text preserved
[L24] in the manuscript tradition is perfectly acceptable and that it would be
[L25] a mistake to emend it, both from a philological and a philosophical perï¿¾spective. In the passage in question, Iamblichus does not merely identify
[L26] two principles of human action (as Wachsmuthâ€™s text would suggest), but
[L27] also defines the relation of subordination between the two, which in turn
[L28] explains the presence within us of an immanent principle ontologically
[L29] dependent upon a superior and transcendent principle.

What â€œfalling into bodiesâ€ looks like in the Stobaean Hermetica (Litwaâ€™s Hermetica II)

Across the Stobaean excerptsâ€”especially the long Isis/Horus materialâ€”embodiment is not treated as a single, uniform metaphysical â€œevent.â€ It is a cluster of related explanations that sometimes sit in tension with each other, and that tension is itself part of what the material contributes: you can watch Hermetic authors (or redactors) trying to hold together (1) a myth of chastening/punishment, (2) a providential cosmology in which embodiment can be a mission, and (3) a quasi-physiological account of how soul is impeded by elemental mixture.

1) The punitive / chastening â€œfallâ€: transgression â†’ binding to bodies


Litwaâ€™s framing of the KorÄ“ Kosmou (SH 23) is explicit that one major strand in this dossier narrates embodiment as the consequence of a transgression: â€œall souls transgressed and were bound to bodies as a punishment.â€ îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn10file3îˆ‚L7-L11îˆ At the same time, he cautions against reading this as a simple â€œtragic mistakeâ€ cosmology: the same tractate can present embodiment as chastening meant to prepare souls for divinely mediated knowledge, not as the result of a jealous creator or an inherently botched world. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn11file2îˆ‚L1-L5îˆ

So the Hermetic â€œfallâ€ here is moral-educational in texture: the body is a disciplining condition that both humbles and trains, and that makes the soul ready for what revelation is going to give later.

2) â€œFallâ€ without punishment: royal souls and embodiment-as-service


SH 24 is invaluable precisely because it qualifies the punitive story. Litwa highlights the correction: although the KorÄ“ Kosmou narrates a universal transgression-punishment pattern, SH 24 introduces â€œroyalâ€ souls who â€œfall into bodiesâ€ but do not experience embodiment as punitive; embodiment can be â€œa service.â€ îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn10file3îˆ‚L7-L11îˆ

This is not a small tweak. It forces you to treat â€œdescentâ€ as a category with internal differentiation: the same metaphysical motionâ€”entry into a bodyâ€”can be read as penal for some, vocational/administrative for others, and it is situated within a cosmic hierarchy of rulers that operates by correspondence (â€œas above, so belowâ€)â€”Craftsman â†’ Sun â†’ Moon â†’ human king. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn10file3îˆ‚L12-L18îˆ

If youâ€™re building a â€œHermetic anthropology,â€ this matters: the tradition is not simply otherworldly escapism. It can also imagine embodied governance as a divinely anchored office.

3) The soulâ€™s reluctance and Fate/necessity: embodiment is not the soulâ€™s natural desire


Another recurrent feature: embodiment is repeatedly framed as something the soul does not want by nature. That line is crucial because it gives the â€œfallâ€ a distinctive Hermetic accent: it is not merely metaphysical descent (as in a neutral emanationist picture), but a kind of forced lodgingâ€”fated rather than naturally appetitive. This is where the â€œfall into bodyâ€ becomes less a one-time cosmogonic drama and more an ongoing condition: embodied life is epistemically and affectively compromising. The fall is re-described as loss of steadiness, not only loss of location.

Finally, the Isis/Horus material that follows the embodiment narrative refuses Epicurean dispersal (â€œsmokeâ€); it insists on the soulâ€™s simplicity/immortality and then maps the afterlife as ascent through levels of air between earth and moon, with gradations that correspond to degrees of soul-quality. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn10file0îˆ‚L8-L19îˆ

That adds an important structural point: the Hermetic â€œfallâ€ is not the last word; the tradition is obsessed with cosmic placement and re-placement, and the body is one station in a larger logistics of souls.

The simplest contrast is that Iamblichus refuses to let descent mean â€œmere punishmentâ€ or â€œmere mistake.â€ But the deeper difference is how descent is integrated into the metaphysics of salvation, ritual, and divine manifestation.

1) The mediating â€œvehicleâ€ (ochÃªma): descent is structurally buffered


A distinctive Iamblichean move is to treat embodiment as mediated by a subtle/aetheric/pneumatic vehicle. A compact way to state the difference

The Stobaean Hermetica give you a plural account of descent: sometimes punitive/chastening, sometimes vocational (royal souls), often fated and experienced as oppression by elemental embodiment. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn10file3îˆ‚L7-L11îˆ îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn10file1îˆ‚L4-L7îˆ îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn10file2îˆ‚L1-L6îˆ Iamblichus, by contrast, systematizes descent through the doctrine of a mediating vehicle and builds a theurgic soteriology in which the â€œsolutionâ€ is not simply release from body but the illumination and reconfiguration of embodied life such that ascent and descent interlockâ€”the soulâ€™s return coinciding with a godâ€™s descent into perceptible/ritual form. îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn9file6îˆ‚L16-L20îˆ îˆ€fileciteîˆ‚turn9file8îˆ‚L15-L20îˆ
